The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Dissecting the Definition of the Syllogism (Qiyās)
Author defines syllogism and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions of a valid syllogism from its definition and distinguish it from arguments that require an external premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism must produce a conclusion directly from its premises without needing an unstated extra premise.

In this passage, the author establishes the central concept of logic: the syllogism. He gives a precise definition that separates true syllogisms from mere sequences of statements. We will unpack each part of this definition to understand what makes an argument a valid syllogism in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (القياس) and the defining phrase (قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر). Underline each component.
The definiens contains three key conditions: composition, admission, and self-sufficient entailment.
2
Examine the condition 'more than one statement'
The phrase 'من أقوال' implies multiple statements. Why does this exclude conversion (العكس) and contraposition (عكس النقيض)? Because those are single-premise inferences.
3
Understand 'mental entailment' (اللزوم الذهني)
The author specifies that the entailment is mental, not real. This means the syllogism does not require actual truth of premises; only that if they were admitted in the mind, the conclusion follows. Why is this important for covering dialectical and sophistical syllogisms?
4
Focus on 'by itself' (لذاته)
This condition excludes arguments that need an external premise. Analyze the equality example: why does 'A equals B, B equals C' not directly yield 'A equals C'? What missing premise is needed?
The missing premise is 'the equal of the equal is equal' – an external premise, so it fails the 'by itself' condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العمدة في الباب هو القياس من هذه الثلاثة؛ إذ هو المعتمد عليه والموثوق به لما أنه يوجب العلم القطعي بخلاف غيره منها. وإنه عبارة عن قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر، والمعنى من الأقوال هو الزائد على القول الواحد وإنه مما يدفع المقدمة الواحدة المستلزمة للعكس المستوي والعكس النقيض وغيرهما؛ ومن اللزوم اللزوم الذهني فإن شعور الذهن بالمتقدمتين مما يستلزم الحكم بالنتيجة سواء كانت كل واحدة من المتقدمتين مسلمة في نفسها، أو لا تكون فالقياس إذن لا يفتقر في الصحة إلى أن تكون كل واحدة منهما مسلمة في نفسها بل يفتقر إلى أنها (١) تكون كل واحدة منهما بحيث لو سلمت في الذهن يلزم من القول المؤلف منهما قول آخر، وحينئذ يندرج في القياس سائر أفراده من الأقيسة البرهانية والجدلية والسوفسطائية وغيرها. وأما قولهم لزم عنه فإنه أعم من اللزوم البين لا محالة فلا جرم يندرج فيه الكامل وغيره ، وقولهم لذاته مما يدفع المفتقر في الإنتاج إلى الغير، وفي اللزوم أيضًا، فإن من الأقوال ما يوهم أن يكون قياسا يفتقر في الإنتاج إلى الغير، وفي اللزوم كذلك. وذلك لا يكون قياسا : أما في الإنتاج فكما إذا قلت «أ» مساو لـ «ب» و «ب» مسـاو لـ «ج»، فيظن أنه ينتج أن (أ) مساو لـ (ج))؛ ولا ينتج ذلك بل يلزم منه أن «أ» مساو لمساوي (ج) و مساوي المساوي مساو لا محالة، فيلزم أن «أ» مساو لـ «ج». (١) النسخة أنه وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
Then the mainstay in this section is the syllogism among these three, for it is the one relied upon and trusted because it necessitates certain knowledge, unlike the others. It is an expression composed of statements which, when admitted, entails by itself another statement. The meaning of 'statements' is more than one statement, and it repels the single premise that implies the converse and the contrapositive and others. As for 'entailment' [here], it is mental entailment, for the mind's awareness of the two premises necessitates the judgment of the conclusion, whether each premise is admitted in itself or not. Therefore, the syllogism does not require for its validity that each premise be admitted in itself, but rather requires that each premise be such that if admitted in the mind, then from the composed statement of the two another statement follows. And then all other types of syllogisms—demonstrative, dialectical, sophistical, and others—are included under syllogism. As for their statement 'entails from it', it is more general than obvious entailment inevitably, so it includes the complete and other than it. And their statement 'by itself' repels that which depends on another for production, and also in entailment. For among statements there are those that give the impression of being a syllogism but depend on another for production, and similarly for entailment. Such is not a syllogism. Regarding production: as when you say 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C', one might think it produces that A is equal to C; but it does not produce that. Rather, it entails that A is equal to the equal of C, and the equal of the equal is necessarily equal, so then it entails that A is equal to C.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition focusing solely on the syllogism (qiyās). The author begins by asserting that the syllogism is the chief among the three types of argument because it yields certain knowledge. He then gives the technical definition: 'an expression composed of statements which, when admitted, entails by itself another statement.' Each part of the definition is unpacked. 'Statements' (aqwāl) means more than one, thereby excluding single-premise inferences like conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawī) and contraposition (‘aks al-naqīḍ). 'Entailment' (luzūm) is mental entailment: the mind's awareness of the premises leads to the judgment of the conclusion. Importantly, the syllogism's validity does not require the premises to be actually true; it only requires that if they were admitted, the conclusion would follow. This allows the definition to cover demonstrative, dialectical, and sophistical syllogisms. The phrase 'by itself' (bi-dhātihi) excludes arguments that need an external premise to produce the conclusion. The author illustrates this with the equality example: 'A equals B, B equals C' does not directly yield 'A equals C' without the additional premise 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other.' Thus, that sequence is not a syllogism because it depends on an extra premise. The footnote indicates a variant reading in the manuscript. This passage provides a rigorous definition of the syllogism essential for subsequent discussions of its figures and moods.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyās): An expression composed of statements which, when admitted, necessitates another statement by itself (i.e., without external support). [Note: In uṣūl al-fiqh, qiyās means analogy; here it is the syllogism, the central argument form in logic.]
لزوم ذهني (luzūm dhihnī): Mental entailment: the mind's awareness of the premises leads to the judgment of the conclusion, regardless of actual truth. [Note: No significant shift; this concept is stable in the tradition.]
لذاته (bi-dhātihi): Indicating that the entailment is self-sufficient, not requiring an additional premise or external factor. [Note: Used consistently in logic to distinguish valid syllogisms from those needing extra premises.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the sequence 'A is equal to B, B is equal to C' not qualify as a syllogism according to the definition?
A) It does not produce a conclusion.
B) It depends on an external premise (equal of equal is equal).
C) It contains more than two premises.
D) The conclusion is not certain.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a syllogism has three essential conditions: multiple statements, mental admission, and self-sufficient entailment without external premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the three conditions in the following argument and determine if it is a valid syllogism according to the definition: 'All intoxicants are forbidden (haram). Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, khamr is forbidden.' Explain why it satisfies the conditions.
?
Consider the argument: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, it has a creator.' Is this a syllogism? Why or why not? (Hint: what type of argument is this?)

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Core Definition of Qiyas and Mental Entailment
Author defines qiyas and explains the meaning of its condition 'lidhatihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential components of the definition of qiyas and explain the concept of mental entailment.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a logical engine that yields a conclusion by itself, regardless of premise truth.

The passage opens by declaring qiyas as the mainstay of logic for yielding certain knowledge. It then provides a precise definition: a composed statement that, when conceded, necessarily entails another statement by itself. This lesson unpacks that definition, especially the concept of mental entailment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the Arabic definition: 'قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر'. Write it down.
2
Break down 'مؤلف من أقوال'
Why must it be composed of multiple statements? What does it exclude? It excludes single-premise inferences like conversion.
3
Examine 'لزم عنه لذاته'
What does 'by itself' mean? The author explains it excludes the need for external conditions or additional premises.
4
Understand mental entailment
The author says the mind's awareness of the premises necessitates the conclusion. How does this differ from requiring true premises?
Mental entailment works even if premises are false.
5
Confirm inclusivity
The definition includes all types of qiyas: demonstrative, dialectical, sophistical, etc.

أن «أ» مساوٍ لمساوي «ج» كان الإشكال باقيا غير أنه لا يكون باقيا فإنه إذا لزم لزم منه لذاته. وأما في اللزوم فإنه لا يفتقر إلى الغير ولا يلزم بما هو اللازم للبعض من المقدمات كما إذا قلت إن جزء الجوهر Then the mainstay in this chapter is the syllogism among these three; for it is the one relied upon and trusted because it yields certain knowledge unlike the others of them. And it is an expression composed of expressions which, when conceded, necessarily entails by itself another expression. The meaning of 'expressions' is more than a single expression, and it excludes the single premise that entails the simple conversion, the contrapositive, and others. And from 'entailment' is mental entailment, for the mind's awareness of the two premises entails the judgment of the conclusion, whether each of the two premises is conceded in itself or not. So the syllogism does not require for validity that each of them be conceded in themselves, but rather requires that each of them be such that if conceded in the mind, the composed expression from them entails another expression. Then all its other individuals, such as demonstrative, dialectical, sophistical syllogisms, etc., are included in the syllogism. As for their saying 'entails from it', it is more general than necessary evident entailment; thus the perfect and other are included. And their saying 'by itself' excludes what is in need of another in producing the conclusion, and also in entailment. For some expressions may appear to be a syllogism that is in need of another in producing the conclusion, and likewise in entailment. And that is not a syllogism. As for in production, for example if you say 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C', one thinks that it produces that A is equal to C; but it does not produce that, rather it entails from it that A is equal to the equal of C, and the equal of the equal is necessarily equal, so it entails that A is equal to C. Then one might say: If it entails from it that A is equal to the equal of C, the difficulty remains; but it does not remain, for if it entails, it entails by itself. As for in entailment, it is not in need of another, nor does it entail what is entailed for some by the premises, as when you say: 'The part of the substance is that which its removal necessitates the removal of the substance, and everything that is not a substance does not necessitate its removal the removal of the substance, so the part of the substance is a substance.' But it does not entail from it; rather it entails from another, namely 'what necessitates its removal the removal of the substance is a substance', so this must be conjoined with it, otherwise it does not entail. And it is not said that without conjoining another with it, it is a syllogism of the second figure exactly; for we say yes, but not with respect to the mentioned conclusion, but with respect to another, namely 'the part of the substance is not what is not a substance'. Some said that their saying 'by itself' excludes a statement like 'he walks at night, so he is a thief' — but it is not so: for that is a syllogism that relies on one of its premises due to the other premise being obvious; and this is among what will be known later. Then their saying 'by itself' yields another benefit: that this necessary consequence does not follow due to a specific matter, such that if it were replaced by something else the necessary consequence would not follow. For example our saying 'Nothing of human is a horse, and every horse is a neighing thing' — the necessary consequence with respect to the state of human and neighing is the negation of neighing from human. And if it were replaced by what is not equivalent to the horse in predication, perhaps the negation would not follow, as if the neighing were replaced by 'animal', the necessary consequence would be the affirmation of animal. And sometimes the word 'necessity' is intended in the definition to exclude this, but there is no need for it. And also, in addition to their saying 'by itself', the word 'not accidentally' is intended to exclude what entailed from it accidentally, like what passed regarding the substance, but it is among what is not needed; for the need for it is in a case where something can entail something else by itself and also accidentally; and that is not possible. And in the example we mentioned, it did not entail by itself, though it entails accidentally. So their saying
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and explains its essential conditions. The author first establishes that qiyas is the main tool in logic because it yields certain knowledge. Then he gives the definition: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl mu'allaf) consisting of statements (aqwal) such that, if they are conceded, another statement follows from them by itself (lidhatihi). He explains each term. 'Statements' means more than one premise, thus distinguishing qiyas from immediate inferences like conversion or contrapositive. The entailment (luzum) is mental: if the mind accepts the premises, it necessarily reaches the conclusion, regardless of whether the premises are actually true. Thus qiyas includes demonstrative, dialectical, and sophistical arguments. The phrase 'entails from it' (lazima 'anhu) is broad enough to cover both evident and non-evident consequences, so the perfect syllogism and others are included. The phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) excludes any argument that requires an additional external term to produce the conclusion or to establish the entailment. He gives an example: 'A is equal to B, B is equal to C' seems to produce 'A is equal to C', but actually it only directly yields 'A is equal to the equal of C'; we need an additional premise that 'the equal of the equal is equal' to get the conclusion. So without that extra premise, it is not a valid qiyas by itself. An objection is raised: if it yields 'A is equal to the equal of C', the difficulty remains because that statement already implies A = C. The answer: the entailment from the premises is indeed by itself, because the mind grasps the implication without external aid. Another example concerns a syllogism that appears to produce a conclusion but the entailment actually depends on an implicit premise: e.g., 'The part of the substance is that whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance; everything that is not a substance does not necessitate the removal of the substance; therefore the part of the substance is a substance.' This does not follow without the additional premise 'what necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance'. Some argue that 'by itself' excludes arguments that implicitly rely on a missing premise, like 'He walks at night, so he is a thief' — this is actually an enthymeme, but it is still a qiyas; the author postpones discussion. The final point: 'by itself' also excludes accidental entailment dependent on specific matter. For example, 'No human is a horse, every horse neighs' yields 'No human neighs' by virtue of the matter; if 'neighs' were replaced with 'animal', the necessary consequence would be positive. So the definition requires that the consequence holds regardless of matter, i.e., formally. The author notes that some add 'out of necessity' or 'not accidentally' to the definition, but these are unnecessary because the definition already covers the intended meaning.

Key Terms
القياس
قول مؤلف من أقوال
لزم عنه لذاته
اللزوم الذهني

According to the passage, what does 'lidhatihi' (by itself) exclude from being a qiyas?
A) Arguments that require an additional premise to produce the conclusion.
B) Arguments with false premises.
C) Arguments that are not demonstrative.
D) Arguments with more than two premises.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a composed statement that entails another statement by itself, distinct from immediate inferences and arguments that rely on external premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Condition 'Lidhatihi' and Exclusion of Dependent Arguments
Author analyzes examples that appear to be syllogisms but fail the 'lidhatihi' condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a true syllogism from an argument that requires an external premise to produce the conclusion.

Core Idea
A valid qiyas must produce its conclusion from the given premises alone.

The passage examines two tricky examples that seem like syllogisms but fail the 'lidhatihi' condition. Understanding these examples sharpens our grasp of why the definition includes 'by itself'.

Guided Path
1
Read the A=B=C example
Premises: A equals B, B equals C. One thinks the conclusion A equals C follows. The author says it produces 'A equals the equal of C', not directly A=C.
2
Identify missing premise
The argument needs the extra premise 'the equal of the equal is equal'. Without it, the conclusion does not follow directly.
3
Examine the substance example
'Part of substance is that whose removal necessitates removal of substance; everything non-substance does not necessitate removal; therefore part of substance is substance.' The author says it needs the premise 'what necessitates removal of substance is substance'.
4
Generalize the pattern
Both examples appear to produce a conclusion but actually depend on an unstated premise. Therefore they are not valid qiyas.
5
Address objection on A=B=C case
One might say: if it entails A equals the equal of C, then by definition of equality, it already implies A=C. But the author insists the direct entailment from the premises is not A=C.

الأقوال هو الزائد على القول الواحد وإنه مما يدفع المقدمة الواحدة المستلزمة للعكس المستوي والعكس النقيض وغيرهما؛ ومن اللزوم اللزوم الذهني فإن شعور الذهن بالمتقدمتين مما يستلزم الحكم بالنتيجة سواء كانت المؤلف منهما قول آخر، وحينئذ يندرج في القياس سائر أفراده من الأقيسة البرهانية والجدلية والسوفسطائية وغيرها. وأما قولهم لزم عنه فإنه أعم من اللزوم البين لا محالة فلا جرم يندرج فيه الكامل وغيره، وقولهم لذاته مما يدفع المفتقر في الإنتاج إلى الغير، وفي اللزوم أيضًا، فإن من الأقوال ما يوهم أن يكون قياسا يفتقر في الإنتاج إلى الغير، وفي اللزوم كذلك. وذلك لا يكون قياسا : أما في الإنتاج فكما إذا قلت «أ» مساو لـ «ب» و «ب» مسـاو لـ «ج»، فيظن أنه ينتج أن (أ) مساو لـ (ج))؛ ولا ينتج ذلك بل يلزم منه أن «أ» مساو لمساوي (ج) و مساوي المساوي مساو لا محالة، فيلزم أن «أ» مساو لـ «ج». ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا لزم منه أن «أ» مساوٍ لمساوي «ج» كان الإشكال باقيا غير أنه لا يكون باقيا فإنه إذا لزم لزم منه لذاته. وأما في اللزوم فإنه لا يفتقر إلى الغير ولا يلزم بما هو اللازم للبعض من المقدمات كما إذا قلت إن جزء الجوهر مما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فلا يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر، فإذن جزء الجوهر جوهر لكنه لا يلزم منه بل يلزم / ومن غيره وهو أن ما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر فهو جوهر، فيجب أن يقرن به هذا وإلا فلا يلزم. ولا يقال إنه من غير أن يقرن به غيره قياس من الشكل الثاني بعينه؛ لأنا نقول نعم لكنه لا بالنسبة إلى النتيجة المذكورة بل بالنسبة إلى الغير وهو أن جزء الجوهر ليس ما ليس بجوهر. ومنهم من قال إن قولهم لذاته» احترازا عن مثل قولنا فلان يطوف بالليل فهو سارق غير أنه لا يكون كذلك: فإن ذلك قياس يقتصر على إحدي مقدّمتيه لظهور المقدّمة الأخرى؛ وهذا من جملة ما يعرف من بعد. ثم إن قولهم لذاته يفيد فائدة أخرى وهو أن هذا اللازم لا يلزمه بسبب مادة مخصوصة حتى لو بدلت بغيرها لم يلزم بذلك اللازم مثل قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ، وكل فرس صهال فاللازم بالنظر إلى حال الإنسان والصهَّال سلب الصهَّال عن الإنسان. ولو بدل بما لا يكون مساويا للفرس في الحمل ربَّما لم يلزم السلب مثل ما إذا بدل الصهال بالحيوان كان اللازم إيجاب الحيوان. وقد يراد في التعريف لفظة الاضطرار احترازًا عن هذا لكن لا حاجة إليه. ويراد أيضًا على قولهم لذاته لفظة لا بالعرض احترازًا عما لزم عنه بالعرض مثل ما مرَّ في الجوهر لكنه من جملة ما لا حاجة إليه؛ إذ الحاجة إليه في حالة يمكن أن يلزم شيء عن شيء لذاته، وبالعرض أيضًا؛ وذلك لا يمكن. وفي مثل ما ذكرنا من المثال لم يلزم لذاته، وإن كان يلزم بالعرض، فقولهم
Then the mainstay in this chapter is the syllogism among these three; for it is the one relied upon and trusted because it yields certain knowledge unlike the others of them. And it is an expression composed of expressions which, when conceded, necessarily entails by itself another expression. The meaning of 'expressions' is more than a single expression, and it excludes the single premise that entails the simple conversion, the contrapositive, and others. And from 'entailment' is mental entailment, for the mind's awareness of the two premises entails the judgment of the conclusion, whether each of the two premises is conceded in itself or not. So the syllogism does not require for validity that each of them be conceded in themselves, but rather requires that each of them be such that if conceded in the mind, the composed expression from them entails another expression. Then all its other individuals, such as demonstrative, dialectical, sophistical syllogisms, etc., are included in the syllogism. As for their saying 'entails from it', it is more general than necessary evident entailment; thus the perfect and other are included. And their saying 'by itself' excludes what is in need of another in producing the conclusion, and also in entailment. For some expressions may appear to be a syllogism that is in need of another in producing the conclusion, and likewise in entailment. And that is not a syllogism. As for in production, for example if you say 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C', one thinks that it produces that A is equal to C; but it does not produce that, rather it entails from it that A is equal to the equal of C, and the equal of the equal is necessarily equal, so it entails that A is equal to C. Then one might say: If it entails from it that A is equal to the equal of C, the difficulty remains; but it does not remain, for if it entails, it entails by itself. As for in entailment, it is not in need of another, nor does it entail what is entailed for some by the premises, as when you say: 'The part of the substance is that which its removal necessitates the removal of the substance, and everything that is not a substance does not necessitate its removal the removal of the substance, so the part of the substance is a substance.' But it does not entail from it; rather it entails from another, namely 'what necessitates its removal the removal of the substance is a substance', so this must be conjoined with it, otherwise it does not entail. And it is not said that without conjoining another with it, it is a syllogism of the second figure exactly; for we say yes, but not with respect to the mentioned conclusion, but with respect to another, namely 'the part of the substance is not what is not a substance'. Some said that their saying 'by itself' excludes a statement like 'he walks at night, so he is a thief' — but it is not so: for that is a syllogism that relies on one of its premises due to the other premise being obvious; and this is among what will be known later. Then their saying 'by itself' yields another benefit: that this necessary consequence does not follow due to a specific matter, such that if it were replaced by something else the necessary consequence would not follow. For example our saying 'Nothing of human is a horse, and every horse is a neighing thing' — the necessary consequence with respect to the state of human and neighing is the negation of neighing from human. And if it were replaced by what is not equivalent to the horse in predication, perhaps the negation would not follow, as if the neighing were replaced by 'animal', the necessary consequence would be the affirmation of animal. And sometimes the word 'necessity' is intended in the definition to exclude this, but there is no need for it. And also, in addition to their saying 'by itself', the word 'not accidentally' is intended to exclude what entailed from it accidentally, like what passed regarding the substance, but it is among what is not needed; for the need for it is in a case where something can entail something else by itself and also accidentally; and that is not possible. And in the example we mentioned, it did not entail by itself, though it entails accidentally. So their saying
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and explains its essential conditions. The author first establishes that qiyas is the main tool in logic because it yields certain knowledge. Then he gives the definition: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl mu'allaf) consisting of statements (aqwal) such that, if they are conceded, another statement follows from them by itself (lidhatihi). He explains each term. 'Statements' means more than one premise, thus distinguishing qiyas from immediate inferences like conversion or contrapositive. The entailment (luzum) is mental: if the mind accepts the premises, it necessarily reaches the conclusion, regardless of whether the premises are actually true. Thus qiyas includes demonstrative, dialectical, and sophistical arguments. The phrase 'entails from it' (lazima 'anhu) is broad enough to cover both evident and non-evident consequences, so the perfect syllogism and others are included. The phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) excludes any argument that requires an additional external term to produce the conclusion or to establish the entailment. He gives an example: 'A is equal to B, B is equal to C' seems to produce 'A is equal to C', but actually it only directly yields 'A is equal to the equal of C'; we need an additional premise that 'the equal of the equal is equal' to get the conclusion. So without that extra premise, it is not a valid qiyas by itself. An objection is raised: if it yields 'A is equal to the equal of C', the difficulty remains because that statement already implies A = C. The answer: the entailment from the premises is indeed by itself, because the mind grasps the implication without external aid. Another example concerns a syllogism that appears to produce a conclusion but the entailment actually depends on an implicit premise: e.g., 'The part of the substance is that whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance; everything that is not a substance does not necessitate the removal of the substance; therefore the part of the substance is a substance.' This does not follow without the additional premise 'what necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance'. Some argue that 'by itself' excludes arguments that implicitly rely on a missing premise, like 'He walks at night, so he is a thief' — this is actually an enthymeme, but it is still a qiyas; the author postpones discussion. The final point: 'by itself' also excludes accidental entailment dependent on specific matter. For example, 'No human is a horse, every horse neighs' yields 'No human neighs' by virtue of the matter; if 'neighs' were replaced with 'animal', the necessary consequence would be positive. So the definition requires that the consequence holds regardless of matter, i.e., formally. The author notes that some add 'out of necessity' or 'not accidentally' to the definition, but these are unnecessary because the definition already covers the intended meaning.

Key Terms
لزم عنه لذاته
افتقر في الإنتاج إلى الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'A equals B, B equals C', what does the author say is the immediate necessary consequence?
A) A equals C
B) A equals the equal of C
C) B equals the equal of A
D) Nothing follows

Key Takeaway
An argument that requires an implicit premise to reach its conclusion is not a genuine syllogism; the conclusion must follow from the stated premises alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden; wine is intoxicating; therefore wine is forbidden.' Explain why this is a valid qiyas according to the definition, using the 'lidhatihi' condition.
?
Examine the argument: 'Zayd is a human; all humans are mortal; therefore Zayd is mortal.' Is this a qiyas? Why? Does it need an external premise?

The Syllogism

5 min
The Scope of Qiyas: Formal vs Material Entailment
Author explains the scope of qiyas and distinguishes formal from material entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of qiyas does not require premises to be true, and why it includes demonstrative, dialectical, and sophistical arguments.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a formal tool; its validity depends on form, not on the truth of its matter.

The passage emphasizes that qiyas does not require true premises, only that if they are conceded, the conclusion follows. This allows qiyas to include arguments in different contexts. Also, the 'lidhatihi' condition ensures the entailment is formal, not material.

Guided Path
1
Note the key statement on truth
The author says: 'القياس لا يفتقر في الصحة إلى أن تكون كل واحدة منهما مسلمة في نفسها' - qiyas does not require each premise to be true in itself.
2
Understand the implication
A syllogism can be valid even with false premises. Example: 'Every animal is a stone; every human is an animal; therefore every human is a stone' is valid in form.
3
Inclusion of all types
The author says all types are included: برهانية, جدلية, سوفسطائية. Qiyas is a neutral logical form.
4
Examine formal vs material example
The example: 'No human is a horse, every horse neighs, therefore no human neighs' depends on the specific matter (neighing). Replace 'neighs' with 'animal', and the conclusion changes.
5
Role of 'lidhatihi' in formality
The 'by itself' condition ensures the consequence holds regardless of matter. This is the formal aspect.

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and explains its essential conditions. The author first establishes that qiyas is the main tool in logic because it yields certain knowledge. Then he gives the definition: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl mu'allaf) consisting of statements (aqwal) such that, if they are conceded, another statement follows from them by itself (lidhatihi). He explains each term. 'Statements' means more than one premise, thus distinguishing qiyas from immediate inferences like conversion or contrapositive. The entailment (luzum) is mental: if the mind accepts the premises, it necessarily reaches the conclusion, regardless of whether the premises are actually true. Thus qiyas includes demonstrative, dialectical, and sophistical arguments. The phrase 'entails from it' (lazima 'anhu) is broad enough to cover both evident and non-evident consequences, so the perfect syllogism and others are included. The phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) excludes any argument that requires an additional external term to produce the conclusion or to establish the entailment. He gives an example: 'A is equal to B, B is equal to C' seems to produce 'A is equal to C', but actually it only directly yields 'A is equal to the equal of C'; we need an additional premise that 'the equal of the equal is equal' to get the conclusion. The answer: the entailment from the premises is indeed by itself, because the mind grasps the implication without external aid. For example, 'No human is a horse, every horse neighs' yields 'No human neighs' by virtue of the matter; if 'neighs' were replaced with 'animal', the necessary consequence would be positive. So the definition requires that the consequence holds regardless of matter, i.e., formally. The author notes that some add 'out of necessity' or 'not accidentally' to the definition, but these are unnecessary because the definition already covers the intended meaning.

Essential Implication in Syllogisms: Refining 'Lidhatihi'
Author refines 'lidhatihi' using part-whole example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an implication in a syllogism is truly essential (lidhatihi) versus when it depends on a hidden premise, using the criterion of needing an external premise.

Core Idea
An implication is essential only if no hidden premise is needed; otherwise it is accidental.

The definition of syllogism requires that the conclusion follow essentially (lidhatihi) from the premises. But what does 'essentially' really exclude? In this passage, the author refines the term by showing that some seemingly necessary inferences actually rely on a missing premise. You will learn to detect such cases and understand why the qualification 'lidhatihi' is crucial for valid reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the objector's challenge
The objector claims that even after adding the phrase 'lidhatihi', the problem of accidental implication remains. Understand the objection: if A equals what equals J, then the conclusion still seems to follow 'essentially', but is it truly essential?
2
Analyze the example of part and essence
Examine the syllogism: 'The part of the essence is that whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence; everything that is not an essence does not necessitate the removal of the essence; therefore the part of the essence is an essence.' This seems to follow, but the author says it does not follow essentially without an extra premise.
The missing premise is 'That whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence is an essence.' Without it, the conclusion does not follow.
3
Identify why the inference is not essential
The conclusion 'the part of the essence is an essence' actually depends on a premise that is not given. The implication is not from the original premises alone, but from an additional hidden premise. Thus, it is not an essential implication of the original premises.
This shows that 'lidhatihi' excludes cases where the implication requires a premise that is not explicitly stated but assumed.
4
Relate to the second-figure syllogism
The author notes that even if we try to reconstruct the inference as a second-figure syllogism, it yields a different conclusion: 'The part of the essence is not that which is not an essence.' This further demonstrates that the original conclusion is not directly entailed.
This reinforces that the missing premise is necessary for the original conclusion.
5
Apply to the night-walking fallacy
The author gives another example: 'So-and-so walks at night, therefore he is a thief.' Here, the conclusion seems to follow only because the premise 'thieves walk at night' is assumed but not stated. This is a suppressed premise fallacy, and 'lidhatihi' is meant to exclude such cases.
Recognize that many everyday arguments rely on hidden premises; 'essentially' demands explicit, complete premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا لزم منه أن «أ» مساوٍ لمساوي «ج» كان الإشكال باقيا غير أنه لا يكون باقيا فإنه إذا لزم لزم منه لذاته. وأما في اللزوم فإنه لا يفتقر إلى الغير ولا يلزم بما هو اللازم للبعض من المقدمات كما إذا قلت إن جزء الجوهر مما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فلا يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر، فإذن جزء الجوهر جوهر لكنه لا يلزم منه بل يلزم من غيره وهو أن ما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر فهو جوهر، فيجب أن يقرن به هذا وإلا فلا يلزم. ولا يقال إنه من غير أن يقرن به غيره قياس من الشكل الثاني بعينه؛ لأنا نقول نعم لكنه لا بالنسبة إلى النتيجة المذكورة بل بالنسبة إلى الغير وهو أن جزء الجوهر ليس ما ليس بجوهر. ومنهم من قال إن قولهم لذاته» احترازا عن مثل قولنا فلان يطوف بالليل فهو سارق غير أنه لا يكون كذلك: فإن ذلك قياس يقتصر على إحدي مقدّمتيه لظهور المقدّمة الأخرى؛ وهذا من جملة ما يعرف من بعد.
Then an objector may say: if it follows from it that 'A' is equal to the equal of 'J', the problem remains, but it does not remain; for if it follows, it follows essentially. As for the implication, it does not need the other, nor does it follow by what is necessary for some of the premises, as when you say: 'The part of the essence is that whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence, and everything that is not an essence does not necessitate the removal of the essence, therefore the part of the essence is an essence.' But it does not follow from it, rather it follows from something else, namely that 'that whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence is an essence', so this must be conjoined to it, otherwise it does not follow. And it cannot be said that it is, without conjoining something else, a syllogism of the second figure exactly; for we say: yes, but not with respect to the mentioned conclusion, but with respect to something else, namely that 'the part of the essence is not that which is not an essence.' And some said that their saying 'essentially' is a safeguard against something like our saying 'So-and-so walks at night, so he is a thief,' but it is not so; for that is a syllogism that is content with one of its two premises because the other premise is obvious; and this is among what will be known later.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the phrase 'lidhatihi' (essentially) in the definition of syllogism. The objector brings up a case where a conclusion follows from a premise via a hidden middle term, making the implication not truly essential. The author argues that such an implication depends on an additional premise that must be conjoined, otherwise the conclusion does not follow. The example given is about the part of the essence (jawhar) and its removal: claiming that the part of the essence is an essence actually relies on a missing premise ('that whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence is an essence'). Without this premise, the inference is invalid. The author further clarifies that even if this inference is considered a second-figure syllogism, it does not yield the original conclusion but a different one. Therefore, the phrase 'lidhatihi' is intended to exclude such accidental implications, including common fallacies like inferring theft from night-walking, where one premise is suppressed. The passage demonstrates the need for strict formal conditions in defining syllogistic implication.

جزء الجوهر

B) That whose removal necessitates the removal of the essence is an essence.

Key Takeaway
An essential implication (lidhatihi) requires that no additional premises—even obvious ones—are needed for the conclusion to follow.

Functions of 'Lidhatihi' in Syllogism Definition
Author explains two benefits of the phrase 'lidhatihi': to exclude material dependency and accidental necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal and material necessity in syllogistic implication and evaluate the redundancy of supplementary phrases in the standard definition.

Core Idea
'Lidhatihi' alone ensures formal necessity; additional safeguards are redundant.

In this passage, the author examines the phrase 'lidhatihi' (essentially) in the definition of syllogism. He argues that this single term already excludes both material dependency (where the implication depends on the specific subject matter) and accidental necessity (where something follows accidentally). The author shows that the added phrases 'idtiraran' (necessarily) and 'la bil-'ard' (not accidentally) are unnecessary because 'lidhatihi' alone suffices. We will work through the logical examples to see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two benefits of 'lidhatihi'
Locate where the author says 'يفيد فائدة أخرى' (conveys another benefit) and 'احترازًا عن هذا' (as a safeguard). The first benefit is to exclude implication that depends on specific matter (madda). The second is to exclude accidental implication (bil-'ard).
These two benefits correspond to the two scenarios where the implication is not from the essence of the premises.
2
Analyze the example of material dependency
Work through the syllogism: 'No human is a horse, and every horse is a neighing being' leads to 'No human is a neighing being'. Now replace 'neighing' with 'animal' in the second premise. The conclusion changes to 'Every human is an animal'. This shows the consequence depends on the specific terms, not just the form.
The author uses this to demonstrate that without 'lidhatihi', the definition would allow material dependency.
3
Assess why 'idtiraran' is unnecessary
The author notes that some add 'idtiraran' (necessarily) to exclude material dependency, but argues it is redundant. Because 'lidhatihi' already implies that the necessity is from the formal structure, not from the particular matter.
Formal necessity does not change when the matter is swapped.
4
Evaluate the need for 'la bil-'ard'
The phrase 'not accidentally' is added to exclude accidental implication. However, the author argues that a thing cannot follow both essentially and accidentally from the same premises, so 'lidhatihi' alone suffices. He refers to an earlier example about the essence where accidental implication could occur, but claims that case is impossible.
Thus, the definition should not include 'la bil-'ard' either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن قولهم لذاته يفيد فائدة أخرى وهو أن هذا اللازم لا يلزمه بسبب مادة مخصوصة حتى لو بدلت بغيرها لم يلزم بذلك اللازم مثل قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وكل فرس صهال فاللازم بالنظر إلى حال الإنسان والصهَّال سلب الصهَّال عن الإنسان. ولو بدل بما لا يكون مساويا للفرس في الحمل ربَّما لم يلزم السلب مثل ما إذا بدل الصهال بالحيوان كان اللازم إيجاب الحيوان. وقد يراد في التعريف لفظة الاضطرار احترازًا عن هذا لكن لا حاجة إليه. ويراد أيضًا على قولهم لذاته لفظة لا بالعرض احترازًا عما لزم عنه بالعرض مثل ما مرَّ في الجوهر لكنه من جملة ما لا حاجة إليه؛ إذ الحاجة إليه في حالة يمكن أن يلزم شيء عن شيء لذاته، وبالعرض أيضًا؛ وذلك لا يمكن. وفي مثل ما ذكرنا من المثال لم يلزم لذاته، وإن كان يلزم بالعرض، فقولهم لذاته يدفع ذلك من غير أن يقرن به لا بالعرض. فمن عرفه بقوله «إنه قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر لا بالعرض اضطرارا»؛ فقد عرفه تعريفا لا يكون كما ينبغي.
Then their saying 'essentially' conveys another benefit: that this necessary consequence does not follow due to a specific matter, so that if it were replaced by something else, it would not follow by that necessary consequence, like our saying 'No human is a horse, and every horse is a neighing being'; the consequence with respect to the state of human and neighing is the negation of neighing from human. And if it were replaced by something not equal to horse in predication, perhaps the negation would not follow, such as when neighing is replaced by animal, the consequence would be the affirmation of animal. And sometimes the word 'necessarily' is intended in the definition as a safeguard against this, but there is no need for it. And also on their saying 'essentially', the phrase 'not accidentally' is intended as a safeguard against what follows accidentally, like what passed regarding the essence, but it is among that which is not needed; for the need for it arises in a case where something can follow from something essentially and also accidentally, and that is impossible. And in the example we mentioned, it did not follow essentially, although it followed accidentally, so their saying 'essentially' repels that without conjoining 'not accidentally'. So whoever defines it by saying 'It is a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessitates by its essence another statement, not accidentally, necessarily' — he has defined it with a definition that is not as it should be.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage expands on the phrase 'lidhatihi' in the definition of syllogism, explaining two additional benefits. First, 'lidhatihi' ensures that the implication is not dependent on the specific subject matter (madda). The author gives an example: 'No human is a horse, and every horse neighs' leads to the conclusion 'No human neighs' — this negation holds because of the specific terms. If the middle term is replaced, e.g., 'animal' instead of 'neighing', the conclusion becomes affirmative: 'Every human is an animal'. So the implication is not purely formal; it depends on the material. The phrase 'lidhatihi' (or alternatively 'idtiraran') is meant to exclude such material dependency, but the author argues it is unnecessary because the definition already implies formal necessity. Second, 'lidhatihi' excludes accidental (bil-'ard) implication, where something follows accidentally from something else, as in the earlier example of the essence. However, the author claims that a situation where something follows both essentially and accidentally is impossible, so this safeguard is also redundant. Thus, defining the syllogism as 'a statement composed of statements that, when accepted, necessitates by its essence another statement, not accidentally, necessarily' is overly elaborate and not ideal.

Key Terms
مادة (madda): The specific subject matter or terms of a syllogism; the content rather than the form. [Note: In later logic, madda refers to the material aspect of propositions; here it is used to distinguish formal from material necessity.]
بالعرض (bil-'ard): Accidentally; the implication holds not due to the essence of the premises but due to an external factor. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
اضطرار (idtirar): Necessity; the implication is forced, not just probable. [Note: Sometimes used interchangeably with 'darura'; here it is considered redundant.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'No human is a horse, every horse neighs → No human neighs', why does the author say the conclusion would change if 'neighs' is replaced by 'animal'?
A) Because the syllogism becomes invalid in the second figure.
B) Because the material of the premise changes, showing the inference is not purely formal.
C) Because 'animal' is not a predicate of horse.
D) Because the conclusion becomes negative instead of affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'lidhatihi' alone suffices to ensure the syllogistic implication is formal and necessary, making additional safeguards redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting Identical Conclusion: The Purpose of a Syllogism is Novelty
Author refutes the objection that a conclusion might be identical to one premise, distinguishing true syllogisms from trivial combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given argument satisfies the essential condition of a syllogism: the conclusion must be distinct from every premise.

Core Idea
A true syllogism produces new knowledge; if the conclusion repeats a premise, it is not a syllogism.

Alas, some have objected that the definition of a syllogism might be violated if the conclusion is identical to one of the premises. The author vigorously refutes this by showing that such cases are not genuine syllogisms. In this lesson, we shall follow his argument and learn how to identify trivial compositions masquerading as syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that in some syllogisms the conclusion is identical to a premise, giving examples like 'Every B is J, every J is H, so every B is H' and claims identity.
The objector's examples appear plausible at first glance but are mistaken.
2
Analyze the counterexamples
Check if the conclusion truly repeats a premise. In 'Every B is J, every J is H, so every B is H', the conclusion is not identical to either premise; it is a new combination. The objection misidentifies the forms.
This step reveals the error in the objection.
3
Understand the criterion
The author states that a syllogistic composition is one 'from which something else is derived' (yustafadu minha ghairuha). This is the essential feature: novelty.
This criterion separates true syllogisms from trivial compositions.
4
Apply the criterion to trivial cases
If someone constructs a composition where the conclusion is exactly one premise (e.g., 'Every A is B, and every B is A, so every A is B'? Actually that's not valid in standard logic), the author says such compositions are not syllogistic because nothing new is derived.
Trivial cases fail the novelty test.
5
Relate to the definition
This explanation clarifies the definition of syllogism, whether conceived as essential (hadd) or accidental (rasm).
The author leaves the definitional type open, but the substance is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم لزم عنه قول آخر [1] فإنه مما يدفع الاتحاد بين النتيجة وبين المقدّمة ما من مقدمات القياس كما في القياس الاقتراني، [ب] أو بينها وبين الكل كما في الاستثنائي. فإن من المحال أن تكون متحدة وهي مجهولة بعد معرفة كل واحدة من المقدّمات متفرقة. ولا يقال كيف تكون مجهولة وهي بنفسها مطلوبة؛ إذ هي مجهولة بالنسبة إلى كل مقدمة من مقدمات القياس وإلا لا تكون بنفسها مطلوبة نحو المقدمات. ولا يبعد أن يخطر ببال أحد ما يناقض هذا من القياسات الاقترانية والاستثنائية فيقال مثلا كل بج»، وكل ج ح» فكل ب ح». أو يقال كل «حج»، وكل «ج ب» فـكـل «ح ب»، فإن النتيجة فيهما عين إحدى مقدمتيه. وعلى هذا إذا قيل كلَّما كان «ج ب» فـ «ح ب لكن جب فح) ب»؛ إذ النتيجة عين إحدي مقدّمتيه. غير أنها وما هو بمثلها من التركيبات لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية فإن القياسية عبارة عما يستفاد منها غيرها؛ وإنها لا تكون كذلك فهذا هو الشرح الواضح للحدّ الذي مرَّ ذكره أو للرسم كما ذهب إليه البعض. فإن منهم من قال «إنه تعريف حدي، ومنهم من قال إنه تعريف رسمي».
And as for their saying 'another statement follows from it' [1], it is what repels the identity between the conclusion and any of the premises of the syllogism, as in the categorical syllogism, [b] or between it and the whole as in the exceptional syllogism. For it is impossible for them to be identical while it is unknown after knowing each of the premises separately. And it cannot be said how it is unknown while it itself is sought; for it is unknown relative to each premise of the syllogism, otherwise it would not itself be sought beyond the premises. And it is not far-fetched that someone might conceive what contradicts this from categorical and exceptional syllogisms, such as saying 'Every B is J, and every J is H, so every B is H' or saying 'Every H is J, and every J is B, so every H is B'; for the conclusion in both is identical to one of the two premises. And similarly, if it is said 'Whenever J is B, then H is B, but J is B, so H is B'; for the conclusion is identical to one of the two premises. However, it and what is like it of compositions are not among syllogistic compositions, for 'syllogistic' means that from which something else is derived; and they are not like that. So this is the clear explanation of the definition that was mentioned, or of the description as some held; for some said 'it is a definition by essential properties' and some said 'it is a definition by accidental properties.'
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage addresses a key requirement in the definition of syllogism: that the conclusion must be distinct from any single premise (or the whole in the case of hypothetical syllogisms). The author argues that if the conclusion were identical to a premise, then after knowing the premises it would already be known, contradicting the purpose of the syllogism which is to produce new knowledge. The objector might bring up examples like 'Every B is J, every J is H, therefore every B is H' where the conclusion seems to repeat a premise? Actually, the author presents counterexamples: in 'Every B is J, every J is H, so every B is H', the conclusion is not identical to either premise. Wait, the author says the conclusion is identical? Let's read carefully: the objector says 'كل ب ج، وكل ج ح فكل ب ح' and claims the conclusion is the same as one premise. But that is false; the conclusion is different. So the author is presenting the objection and then refuting it by clarifying that such compositions are not truly syllogistic because they do not produce a new result. The author states that the mark of a syllogism is that something else is derived (yustafadu minha ghairuha). So the examples given, if taken as syllogisms, would be trivial and are excluded. The passage concludes by noting that this explanation clarifies the earlier definition, whether it is considered a hadd (essential definition) or rasm (accidental description).

Key Terms

القياس الاستثنائي
تعريف حدي / رسمي

The mark of a genuine syllogism is that it yields new knowledge; without novelty, the composition is not truly syllogistic.

Critique of the Definition of Qiyas and the Distinct Conclusion Condition
Author critiques a definition of qiyas and explains distinct conclusion condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the sufficiency of the definition of qiyas by identifying the condition that the conclusion must be distinct from the premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must produce a conclusion distinct from any premise.

In this passage, the author critiques a widely accepted definition of qiyas (syllogism). He argues that the definition fails to exclude cases where the conclusion is identical to a premise, which would not yield new knowledge. Understanding this critique helps you evaluate definitions of syllogism in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Find the definition of qiyas that the author examines: 'a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessarily entail another statement, not accidentally.' This definition is common but incomplete.
2
Understand the condition of distinct conclusion
The author argues that the conclusion must be distinct from the premises. If the conclusion is identical to a premise, the syllogism does not produce new knowledge and is not a true syllogism.
This condition is captured by the phrase 'luzum' (necessary entailment) and the requirement that the conclusion is 'ghayr ma'lum' (unknown) prior to the syllogism.
3
Examine the examples given
The author provides examples where the conclusion appears identical to a premise: e.g., 'Every B is J, every J is H, so every B is H' – but note that in some arrangements the conclusion may be the same as a premise.
4
Recognize why these examples fail
Such compositions are not truly syllogistic because they do not yield something other than the premises. A genuine syllogism must produce new knowledge.
The author emphasizes that the definition must exclude these cases.
5
Sum up the corrected definition
The corrected definition of qiyas must include that the conclusion is necessarily entailed and is distinct from the premises. This can be a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm) depending on the scholar's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن عرفه بقوله «إنه قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر لا بالعرض اضطرارا»؛ فقد عرفه تعريفا لا يكون كما ينبغي. وأما قولهم لزم عنه قول آخر فإنه مما يدفع الاتحاد بين النتيجة وبين المقدّمة ما من مقدمات القياس كما في القياس الاقتراني، أو بينها وبين الكل كما في الاستثنائي. فإن من المحال أن تكون متحدة وهي مجهولة بعد معرفة كل واحدة من المقدّمات متفرقة. ولا يقال كيف تكون مجهولة وهي بنفسها مطلوبة؛ إذ هي مجهولة بالنسبة إلى كل مقدمة من مقدمات القياس وإلا لا تكون بنفسها مطلوبة نحو المقدمات. ولا يبعد أن يخطر ببال أحد ما يناقض هذا من القياسات الاقترانية والاستثنائية فيقال مثلا كل ب ج، وكل ج ح فكل ب ح. أو يقال كل ح ج، وكل ج ب فكل ح ب، فإن النتيجة فيهما عين إحدى مقدمتيه. وعلى هذا إذا قيل كلَّما كان ج ب ف ح ب لكن جب فح ب؛ إذ النتيجة عين إحدي مقدّمتيه. غير أنها وما هو بمثلها من التركيبات لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية فإن القياسية عبارة عما يستفاد منها غيرها؛ وإنها لا تكون كذلك فهذا هو الشرح الواضح للحدّ الذي مرَّ ذكره أو للرسم كما ذهب إليه البعض. فإن منهم من قال «إنه تعريف حدي، ومنهم من قال إنه تعريف رسمي».
So whoever defines it by saying, 'It is a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessarily entail by themselves another statement, not accidentally,' has defined it with a definition that is not as it should be. As for their statement 'entails another statement,' it repels the unity between the conclusion and any one of the premises of the syllogism, as in the conjunctive syllogism, or between it and the whole, as in the exceptive syllogism. For it is impossible for it to be unified while being unknown after knowing each of the premises separately. And it is not said how it can be unknown while it is itself sought? For it is unknown relative to each premise of the syllogism; otherwise it would not be itself sought from the premises. And it is not unlikely that someone might think of something that contradicts this from conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms. For example, it is said: 'Every B is J, and every J is H, so every B is H.' Or it is said: 'Every H is J, and every J is B, so every H is B.' For the conclusion in both is identical to one of their premises. And likewise if it is said: 'Whenever J is B, then H is B; but J is B, so H is B.' For the conclusion is identical to one of its two premises. However, such compositions and what is like them are not among syllogistic compositions, for syllogistic is what yields something other than them; and they are not so. So this is the clear explanation of the definition that was mentioned, or of the description as some have held. For among them are those who say it is a definition by complete definition (hadd), and among them are those who say it is a definition by description (rasm).
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage critiques a specific definition of qiyas (syllogism) that is commonly given: 'a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessarily entail by themselves another statement, not accidentally.' The author argues that this definition is insufficient because it fails to exclude cases where the conclusion is identical to one of the premises. He explains that the definition must ensure that the conclusion is distinct and unknown before the syllogism. He gives examples of syllogisms where the conclusion appears to be the same as a premise, such as 'Every B is J, every J is H, so every B is H' – though note that the claim of identity in this example is problematic; the author may be referring to a different arrangement. He then states that such compositions are not truly syllogistic because a syllogism must yield something other than its premises. Thus, the definition is clarified as a complete definition (hadd) or description (rasm) depending on one's view.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): A statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessarily entail by themselves another statement, not accidentally, with the condition that the conclusion is distinct from the premises. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
نتيجة (natija): The conclusion that follows necessarily from the premises of a syllogism, which must be unknown prior to the syllogism. [Note: No significant shift.]
لزوم (luzum): The necessary entailment that occurs when premises are accepted, leading to the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the definition 'a statement composed of statements that entail another statement, not accidentally' insufficient?
A) Because it excludes conjunctive syllogisms.
B) Because it does not require the conclusion to be distinct from the premises.
C) Because it includes cases where the entailment is accidental.
D) Because it requires the conclusion to be known beforehand.

Key Takeaway
A correct definition of qiyas must include that the conclusion follows necessarily and is distinct from the premises.

Mental Implication and the Cause of Inference in Qiyas
Author discusses mental implication, defines fikra, and argues about the cause of the conclusion being the totality or individual premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the cause of the conclusion in a syllogism is neither the totality of premises nor any single premise, and identify the role of the social form (hay'a ijtima'iyya).

Core Idea
The cause of a syllogistic conclusion is neither the sum of premises nor any one alone, but their structured combination.

This passage delves into the nature of mental implication in syllogisms. It defines 'thought' (fikra) as an arrangement of known things that yields new knowledge, and then investigates what actually causes the conclusion: is it all premises together, or each individually? The author refutes both options and addresses an objection about enthymemes, leading to a discussion of the 'social form' (hay'a ijtima'iyya) of the premises. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping how syllogisms truly produce knowledge in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish mental from verbal implication
The author emphasizes that the necessary implication is mental (luzum dhihni), not verbal. Uttering premises does not force the conclusion to be uttered; the implication occurs in the mind.
2
Define fikra (thought)
Fikra is defined as an arrangement of known things in a particular way such that from their occurrence in the mind, knowledge of something else necessarily follows. Grasp that fikra is an active cognitive process.
3
Identify the two candidate causes
The cause (muqtada) of obtaining knowledge of the conclusion could be either all premises together (totality) or each premise individually.
4
Analyze the refutation of the totality view
The totality cannot be the cause because (1) the cause must be present when the effect occurs, but thought is a seeking, which cannot occur at the same time as the effect (since seeking stops when the effect is obtained); (2) the mind cannot hold all premises simultaneously – when it attends to one, it turns away from others, so they are not present as a unit to cause anything.
5
Analyze the refutation of the individual premise view
If each premise were independently sufficient, then one premise alone should produce the conclusion, making the rest superfluous. Moreover, multiple independent causes for one effect are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ اللزوم اللازم من التسليم كما مر هو اللزوم الذهني، إنه من الأفكار النفسانية لا من الأقوال اللسانية. فإن من تلفظ بمقدّمات القياس لا يلزمه التلفظ بالنتيجة، «والفكرة» عبارة عن ترتيب أمور معلومة ترتيباً خاصا يلزم من حصولها في الذهن حصول علم بشيء آخر على حسب العلم بتلك الأمور. ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول فيه المقتضى الحصول العلم بالنتيجة في الذهن لا يخلو من أن يكون جميع تلك العلوم، أو كل واحد منها والأول باطل؛ إذ المقتضى لحصول الشيء يمكن أن يحصل حال حصول ذلك الشيء؛ وما نحن فيه وهو الفكر لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك؛ إذ هو طلب، وطلب الشيء حال حصول ذلك الشيء محال. ولأن المقتضى وهو مجموع تلك الأمور المعلومة في الذهن دفعة ممتنع الحصول فإن الذهن متى توجه إلى واحد من تلك الأمور أعرض عن الباقي، ومتى توجه إلى الباقي أعرض عنه فكانت تلك الأمور معدومة في الذهن من حيث هي تلك الأمور، ولو كانت معدومة فلا يمكن تأثيرها في وجود الشيء. والثاني باطل أيضًا؛ إذ البعض منها لا يكون مستقلا في الاقتضاء إلا وأن يكون الباقي ضايعا. وكذلك فكان التعرض للبعض منها ضايعا لا فائدة فيه ؛ وفيه من الفساد ما فيه. ولأن كل واحد منها إذا كان مستقلا بالاقتضاء يلزم أن يجتمع على المعلول الواحد علل مستقلة؛ وذلك لا يمكن فإن من المحال أن يتحقق الشيء بهذا وبغيره كذلك. ولقائل أن يقول فيه كيف هو وقد كانت المقدّمة الواحدة بنفسها منتجة في مثل قولنا فلان يتحرك فهو حي لكنا نقول : قد مر من قبل أن تلك المقدمة بنفسها لا تكون منتجة بل المنتج مجموع هاتين المقدمتين وهو قولنا فلان يتحرك، وكل من يتحرك فهو حي، فهو إذن حي. وقد يقال أيضًا إذا لم يكن كل واحد من تلك الأمور الحاضرة في الذهن مؤثرًا في حصول الأثر وهو النتيجة لم يكن القياس مؤثرًا لذاته؛ وذلك لأن المؤثرية مفتقرة إلى الهيئة الاجتماعية حينئذ، والهيئة الاجتماعية غير القياس لا محالة لما أنها لا تخلو من أن تكون داخلة في نفس القياس أو لا تكون. فإن كانت داخلاة فهو جزؤه بالضرورة، والجزء غير الكل. فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء لا مع الزائد عين الشيء مع الزائد. وإن لم تكن داخلة فهو خارجة بالضرورة، والخارج عن نفس الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون عين ذلك الشيء.
Then the necessary implication from acceptance, as was mentioned, is mental implication; it is from psychological thoughts, not from verbal statements. For whoever utters the premises of a syllogism is not thereby compelled to utter the conclusion. 'Thought' (fikra) is an arrangement of known things in a particular way such that from their occurrence in the mind, knowledge of something else necessarily follows, according to the knowledge of those things. Then an objector may say: The cause of obtaining knowledge of the conclusion in the mind cannot be other than either all those sciences (i.e., all the premises) or each one of them. The first is false: because the cause of something can occur at the time of that thing's occurrence; but what we are dealing with, namely thought, cannot be like that because it is a seeking, and seeking a thing at the time of its occurrence is impossible. And because the cause, which is the totality of those known things, occurring simultaneously in the mind is impossible to obtain, for when the mind attends to one of those things it turns away from the rest, and when it attends to the rest it turns away from it, so those things are nonexistent in the mind as those things; and if they were nonexistent they cannot effect the existence of the thing. The second is also false: because some of them cannot be independent in causation unless the rest are superfluous. Likewise, focusing on some of them is superfluous and of no benefit, and it contains what corruption it contains. And because if each one of them were independent in causation, then independent causes would concur on one effect; that is impossible, for it is impossible for a thing to be realized by this and by another in the same way. And an objector may say: How can that be when a single premise is by itself productive in such a statement as 'Zaid moves, therefore he is alive'? But we say: It has been stated before that a single premise is not productive by itself, but rather the producer is the combination of these two premises, namely our statement 'Zaid moves, and everything that moves is alive; therefore he is alive'. And it may also be said: If not every one of those things present in the mind is effective in producing the effect, namely the conclusion, then the syllogism is not effective by itself. This is because effectiveness then depends on the social form (hay'a ijtima'iyya), and the social form is necessarily other than the syllogism, since it cannot be other than either being inside the syllogism itself or not. If it is inside, then it is a part of it necessarily, and the part is not the whole. For it is impossible for a thing without the addition to be the same as a thing with the addition. And if it is not inside, then it is outside necessarily, and what is outside the thing itself cannot be that thing.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage examines the nature of mental implication in syllogisms. The author first clarifies that the necessary implication is mental, not verbal: uttering premises does not require uttering the conclusion. He then defines 'thought' (fikra) as a particular arrangement of known things that yields knowledge of something else. The central discussion concerns the cause of the conclusion: is it all premises together or each individually? The author argues against both. The totality cannot be the cause because the mind cannot hold all premises simultaneously; when focused on one, it ignores others, so they are effectively absent. Each premise individually cannot be the cause because then only one would suffice, making the rest superfluous, and multiple independent causes for one effect are impossible. An objection is raised: a single premise like 'Zaid moves, therefore he is alive' seems productive. The response is that this is actually an enthymeme with an implicit premise: 'Everything that moves is alive.' Thus the cause is the combination. Finally, the author addresses the role of the 'social form' (hay'a ijtima'iyya) – the collective arrangement – and argues that if it is part of the syllogism, it is not the whole; if external, it cannot be identical to the syllogism.

Key Terms
فكرة
لزوم ذهني
مقتضى
هيئة اجتماعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the premises are not all known.
D) Because the conclusion is already known.

Key Takeaway
The cause of a syllogistic conclusion is the structured combination of premises, not any single premise nor their mere aggregate.

The Syllogism
