The Natural Figure: Why the First Figure is Supreme
Author explains why the first figure is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify the superiority of the first figure over the other three figures using its naturalness and ability to produce the universal affirmative, with examples from Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The first figure is the natural figure because it alone produces all conclusion types, including the most noble universal affirmative.

In this lesson, we examine why the classical logicians ranked the first figure as the highest. The author argues that its arrangement mirrors the natural movement of the mind, and that it is the only figure capable of yielding the universal affirmative proposition, which is the most valuable type of conclusion in demonstrative reasoning. This understanding is crucial for evaluating the strength of syllogistic arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural characteristics
What textual features indicate that the first figure is 'natural'? Look for the author's description of how the mind moves through the terms.
2
List the four conclusion types
The author states the first figure produces all four types. Name them in Arabic and English.
3
Explain the supremacy of the universal affirmative
Why is the universal affirmative considered the best conclusion? Relate to its role in Islamic demonstration (burhan).
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Construct a first figure syllogism in fiqh that yields a universal affirmative, confirming that only this figure can do so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشكل الأول من الأشكال الأربعة فإنه إنما يتعين لكونه أولا لأن إنتاجه بين بنفسه وقياساته كاملة، وترتيبه طبيعي لما أن الذهن ينتقل فيه من الأصغر إلى الأوسط ومن الأوسط إلى الأكبر. ولا ينتج جميع المطالب وهي الموجبة الكلية والجزئية والسالبة الكلية والجزئية. ثم أفضل هذه المطالب هو الموجبة الكلية؛ وذلك لا يحصل إلا من هذا الشكل. وأما الثاني منها فذلك إنما يتعين لكونه ثانيا لأنه يشارك الشكل الطبيعي وهو الأول في أشرف المقدمتين وهي الصغرى على خلاف الثالث، فإنه يشارك الطبيعي في غيرها وهي الكبرى. ولأن ما ينتجه الثاني؛ وهو السالبة الكلية، فإنه أشرف ما ينتجه الثالث؛ وهو الموجبة الجزئية. ولا يقال كيف هو والكبرى أشرف من الصغرى لما أن القياس لا يتم إلا بالكبرى. وكذلك الموجبة الجزئية عن السالبة الكلية؛ إذ الموجبة تدل على وجود الشيء بخلاف السالبة. فإن القياس إنما يتم بالكبرى لكونها تالية للصغرى؛ وذلك يدل على تقدم الصغرى صورة ومعنى. ولأن الصغرى أشرف لكونها مشتملة على الأشرف وهو موضوع النتيجة. وقد علمت بأن الموضوع أشرف من المحمول. وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها وإن كانت تدل على وجود الشيء فلا تكون في قوة السالبة الكلية لما أن السالبة الكلية كالضد للموجبة الكلية، وضد الشيء في قوة ذلك الشيء. ولأنها منافية للموجبة الجزئية والكلية على خلاف الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تكون منافية للسالبة الجزئية والكلية، ولما كانت السالبة الكلية أقوى كانت أشرف وأفضل. وأما الثالث منها فإنه إنما يتعين لكونه تاليا ومقدما على الرابع لأنه يوافق الشكل الطبيعي في إحدى مقدميه بخلاف الرابع فإنه يخالف الطبيعي في كل واحدة من مقدمتيه. ولهذا يكون بعيدا عن الطبع ويحتاج في إبانة ما يلزم منه إلى زيادة تفكر وتأمل. والثالث وإن لم يكون لزوم ما يلزم عنه بينا بذاته فلا يكون بعيدا عن الطبع بل قريبا منه يمكن أن يفهم قياسيته قبل البيان لشيء آخر، لمن كان له قارحة وقارة بين ذهنه إلى ما يلزم من غيره من غير أن ينظر فيما يبينه من الأمور ويقرره. ولهذا لم يتعرض السلف للشكل الرابع وإن كان هو من اللوازم في القسمة وإلا لا تكون القسمة عقلية بل يتعرضون للأشكال الثلاثة.
As for the first figure among the four figures, it is determined to be first because its conclusion is evident by itself and its syllogisms are perfect, and its arrangement is natural because the mind moves in it from the minor to the middle and from the middle to the major. And it produces all demands, i.e., universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. Then the best of these demands is the universal affirmative; and that is obtained only from this figure. As for the second of them, it is determined to be second because it shares with the natural figure – which is the first – the more honorable of the two premises, i.e., the minor, unlike the third, for it shares with the natural figure the other premise, i.e., the major. Also because what the second produces – i.e., the universal negative – is more honorable than what the third produces – i.e., the particular affirmative. And it is not said how is that, since the major is more honorable than the minor? Because the syllogism is only completed by the major. Similarly, the particular affirmative is [less honorable] than the universal negative; for the affirmative indicates the existence of a thing, unlike the negative. But the syllogism is completed by the major because it follows the minor; and that indicates the priority of the minor in form and meaning. And because the minor is more honorable because it contains the more honorable – i.e., the subject of the conclusion – and you have known that the subject is more honorable than the predicate. As for the particular affirmative, even though it indicates the existence of a thing, it is not in the strength of the universal negative, because the universal negative is like the opposite of the universal affirmative, and the opposite of a thing is in its strength. And because it contradicts the particular affirmative and the universal, unlike the particular affirmative, for it does not contradict the particular negative and the universal negative. And when the universal negative is stronger, it is more honorable and better. As for the third of them, it is determined to be third and preceding the fourth because it agrees with the natural figure in one of its premises, unlike the fourth, for it disagrees with the natural figure in each of its two premises. Hence it is far from nature and requires in clarifying what follows from it additional thought and reflection. The third, even if the necessity of what follows from it is not evident by itself, is not far from nature but near it, so that one can understand its syllogistic form before the explanation of something else, for one who has a sharp mind and a clear understanding, from his intellect to what follows from it without looking into what explains it and establishes it. For this reason, the predecessors (salaf) did not deal with the fourth figure, even though it is among the necessary [divisions] in the division, otherwise the division would not be rational. Rather they only dealt with the three figures.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage ranks the four figures of the categorical syllogism, explaining why the first is most noble, then the second, then the third, and why the fourth is neglected. The first figure is called natural (tabi‘i) because the mind naturally moves from the minor term through the middle to the major. It alone produces all four proposition types, and it is the only figure that yields the most honored conclusion: the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram'). The second figure is ranked second because it shares the minor premise with the first figure (the minor is considered more honorable as it contains the subject of the conclusion) and because it produces the universal negative (e.g., 'No intoxicant is halal'), which is stronger than the particular affirmative produced by the third figure. The author defends the honor of the minor over the major by noting the syllogism depends on the major but the minor has formal priority and contains the subject. The third figure is weaker but still natural because it agrees with the first figure in one premise; its conclusions are not always evident but need only slight reflection. The fourth figure is far from nature, disagreeing in both premises; thus the early scholars (salaf) did not treat it, although it is a logical necessity for a complete division. This ranking guides the logician to prefer the first figure for certain demonstrations (burhan) and to understand the relative strength of syllogistic forms.

Key Terms
الشكل الطبيعي
الإنتاج
الموجبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Hierarchy of Figures: Why the Second is above the Third
Author explains why the second figure is superior to the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the second and third figures and explain why the second is ranked higher based on the type of conclusion and the honor of the premises.

Core Idea
The second figure is nobler than the third because it produces the stronger universal negative and shares the more honorable minor premise with the first figure.

After establishing the first figure's supremacy, the author turns to the second and third figures. He argues that the second figure is superior because it participates with the natural figure in the more honorable premise (the minor) and because it yields the universal negative, which is stronger than the particular affirmative produced by the third figure. Understanding this ranking helps us appreciate the relative strength of different syllogistic forms in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Compare premise sharing
Identify which premise each figure (second and third) shares with the first figure.
2
Assess the honor of the minor premise
Why does the author consider the minor premise more honorable? Look for the argument about the subject of the conclusion.
3
Compare conclusion strength
The second figure produces universal negative, the third produces particular affirmative. Why is universal negative stronger? Note the author's reasoning about opposites and contradiction.
4
Construct examples
Build a second figure syllogism from fiqh or kalam and a third figure syllogism, then compare their conclusions.

The Third and Fourth Figures: Natural Weakness vs. Unnatural Neglect
Author explains the status of the third and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the third figure is considered closer to nature than the fourth, and why the fourth figure was neglected by early Muslim logicians despite being logically necessary.

Core Idea
The third figure is near the natural figure because it agrees in one premise, while the fourth is far from nature and requires excessive reflection, hence the salaf ignored it.

In the final part of the passage, the author discusses the third and fourth figures. He explains that the third figure, though not self-evident, is still close to the natural figure because it shares one premise with it. In contrast, the fourth figure disagrees with the natural figure in both premises, making it 'far from nature' and demanding additional thought. This unnaturalness led the early scholars (salaf) to omit the fourth figure from their treatments, even though it is a necessary division for a complete classification. This lesson explores why naturalness matters in Islamic logical pedagogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify agreement with the natural figure
How many premises does each figure (third and fourth) share with the first figure?
2
Understand 'far from nature'
What does the author mean by 'ba'idan 'an al-tab'? Why would that make a figure difficult to use?
3
Analyze the salaf's neglect
Why did the predecessors ignore the fourth figure? What is the logical necessity of including it?
4
Find a third figure example
Identify a syllogism in kalam or fiqh that uses the third figure (e.g., specific premises with middle as subject in both).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشكل الأول من الأشكال الأربعة فإنه إنما يتعين لكونه أولا لأن إنتاجه بين بنفسه وقياساته كاملة، وترتيبه طبيعي لما أن الذهن ينتقل فيه من الأصغر إلى الأوسط ومن الأوسط إلى الأكبر. ولا ينتج جميع المطالب وهي الموجبة الكلية والجزئية والسالبة الكلية والجزئية. ثم أفضل هذه المطالب هو الموجبة الكلية؛ وذلك لا يحصل إلا من هذا الشكل. وأما الثاني منها فذلك إنما يتعين لكونه ثانيا لأنه يشارك الشكل الطبيعي وهو الأول في أشرف المقدمتين وهي الصغرى على خلاف الثالث، فإنه يشارك الطبيعي في غيرها وهي الكبرى. ولأن ما ينتجه الثاني؛ وهو السالبة الكلية، فإنه أشرف ما ينتجه الثالث؛ وهو الموجبة الجزئية. ولا يقال كيف هو والكبرى أشرف من الصغرى لما أن القياس لا يتم إلا بالكبرى. وكذلك الموجبة الجزئية عن السالبة الكلية؛ إذ الموجبة تدل على وجود الشيء بخلاف السالبة. فإن القياس إنما يتم بالكبرى لكونها تالية للصغرى؛ وذلك يدل على تقدم الصغرى صورة ومعنى. ولأن الصغرى أشرف لكونها مشتملة على الأشرف وهو موضوع النتيجة. وقد علمت بأن الموضوع أشرف من المحمول. وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها وإن كانت تدل على وجود الشيء فلا تكون في قوة السالبة الكلية لما أن السالبة الكلية كالضد للموجبة الكلية، وضد الشيء في قوة ذلك الشيء. ولأنها منافية للموجبة الجزئية والكلية على خلاف الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تكون منافية للسالبة الجزئية والكلية، ولما كانت السالبة الكلية أقوى كانت أشرف وأفضل. وأما الثالث منها فإنه إنما يتعين لكونه تاليا ومقدما على الرابع لأنه يوافق الشكل الطبيعي في إحدى مقدميه بخلاف الرابع فإنه يخالف الطبيعي في كل واحدة من مقدمتيه. ولهذا يكون بعيدا عن الطبع ويحتاج في إبانة ما يلزم منه إلى زيادة تفكر وتأمل. والثالث وإن لم يكون لزوم ما يلزم عنه بينا بذاته فلا يكون بعيدا عن الطبع بل قريبا منه يمكن أن يفهم قياسيته قبل البيان لشيء آخر، لمن كان له قارحة وقارة بين ذهنه إلى ما يلزم من غيره من غير أن ينظر فيما يبينه من الأمور ويقرره. ولهذا لم يتعرض السلف للشكل الرابع وإن كان هو من اللوازم في القسمة وإلا لا تكون القسمة عقلية بل يتعرضون للأشكال الثلاثة.
As for the first figure among the four figures, it is determined to be first because its conclusion is evident by itself and its syllogisms are perfect, and its arrangement is natural because the mind moves in it from the minor to the middle and from the middle to the major. And it produces all demands, i.e., universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. Then the best of these demands is the universal affirmative; and that is obtained only from this figure. As for the second of them, it is determined to be second because it shares with the natural figure – which is the first – the more honorable of the two premises, i.e., the minor, unlike the third, for it shares with the natural figure the other premise, i.e., the major. Also because what the second produces – i.e., the universal negative – is more honorable than what the third produces – i.e., the particular affirmative. And it is not said how is that, since the major is more honorable than the minor? Because the syllogism is only completed by the major. Similarly, the particular affirmative is [less honorable] than the universal negative; for the affirmative indicates the existence of a thing, unlike the negative. But the syllogism is completed by the major because it follows the minor; and that indicates the priority of the minor in form and meaning. And because the minor is more honorable because it contains the more honorable – i.e., the subject of the conclusion – and you have known that the subject is more honorable than the predicate. As for the particular affirmative, even though it indicates the existence of a thing, it is not in the strength of the universal negative, because the universal negative is like the opposite of the universal affirmative, and the opposite of a thing is in its strength. And because it contradicts the particular affirmative and the universal, unlike the particular affirmative, for it does not contradict the particular negative and the universal negative. And when the universal negative is stronger, it is more honorable and better. As for the third of them, it is determined to be third and preceding the fourth because it agrees with the natural figure in one of its premises, unlike the fourth, for it disagrees with the natural figure in each of its two premises. Hence it is far from nature and requires in clarifying what follows from it additional thought and reflection. The third, even if the necessity of what follows from it is not evident by itself, is not far from nature but near it, so that one can understand its syllogistic form before the explanation of something else, for one who has a sharp mind and a clear understanding, from his intellect to what follows from it without looking into what explains it and establishes it. For this reason, the predecessors (salaf) did not deal with the fourth figure, even though it is among the necessary [divisions] in the division, otherwise the division would not be rational. Rather they only dealt with the three figures.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage ranks the four figures of the categorical syllogism, explaining why the first is most noble, then the second, then the third, and why the fourth is neglected. The first figure is called natural (tabi‘i) because the mind naturally moves from the minor term through the middle to the major. It alone produces all four proposition types, and it is the only figure that yields the most honored conclusion: the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram'). The second figure is ranked second because it shares the minor premise with the first figure (the minor is considered more honorable as it contains the subject of the conclusion) and because it produces the universal negative (e.g., 'No intoxicant is halal'), which is stronger than the particular affirmative produced by the third figure. The author defends the honor of the minor over the major by noting the syllogism depends on the major but the minor has formal priority and contains the subject. The third figure is weaker but still natural because it agrees with the first figure in one premise; its conclusions are not always evident but need only slight reflection. The fourth figure is far from nature, disagreeing in both premises; thus the early scholars (salaf) did not treat it, although it is a logical necessity for a complete division. This ranking guides the logician to prefer the first figure for certain demonstrations (burhan) and to understand the relative strength of syllogistic forms.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the early logicians (salaf) not deal with the fourth figure according to the passage?
A) Because it is logically invalid.
B) Because it is far from nature and requires excessive reflection.
C) Because it produces only negative conclusions.
D) Because it was discovered later.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure is a logical necessity for complete division but was neglected because of its unnaturalness; the third figure, though weaker, remains close to natural reasoning.

The Syllogism

Why the Second Figure is Superior to the Third
Author argues the second figure is superior to the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism and explain why the second is considered superior based on premise honor and conclusion strength.

Core Idea
The second figure is superior because it shares the minor premise with the first and yields a stronger universal negative.

In the Islamic logical tradition, not all syllogistic figures are equal. The second figure is often preferred over the third because it shares the more honorable premise with the first figure and produces a more powerful conclusion. In this passage, the author explains this preference by analyzing which premises are more honorable and why the universal negative conclusion outweighs the particular affirmative. Understanding this hierarchy deepens your appreciation of how logicians evaluate arguments for certainty and persuasiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figures being compared
The second figure (al-shakl al-thani) and the third figure (al-shakl al-thalith) are being compared. The author also references the first figure as the 'natural figure' (al-shakl al-tabi'i).
2
Focus on the premise honor criterion
The author claims the second figure shares the more honorable premise—the minor (al-sughra)—with the first figure, while the third shares the less honorable premise—the major (al-kubra). Understand why the minor is considered more honorable: it contains the subject of the conclusion, which is more honorable than the predicate.
The minor premise is 'more honorable' because it contains the subject of the conclusion, and the subject is considered more noble than the predicate.
3
Analyze the conclusion strength criterion
The second figure produces a universal negative conclusion (saliba kulliyya), while the third produces a particular affirmative (mujiba juz'iyya). The author argues the universal negative is stronger because it is contrary to both the universal and particular affirmative, and it is the opposite of the universal affirmative. A thing's opposite has the same strength.
The author uses a logical principle: the opposite of a thing is in the strength of that thing.
4
Address the objection
A potential objection is raised: if the major premise is more honorable because the syllogism is only completed by it, why is the second figure superior? The author responds by reaffirming the priority of the minor premise in form and meaning, and by elaborating on the strength of the universal negative over the particular affirmative.
The author's response shows that the criterion of 'completion' does not override the inherent honor of the minor premise.
5
Synthesize the superiority argument
Combine the two criteria: the second figure is superior because (a) it shares the more honorable minor premise and (b) it produces the stronger universal negative conclusion. The third figure fails on both counts.
This synthesis is the core of the author's comparative analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني منها فذلك إنما يتعين لكونه ثانيًا لأنه يشارك الشكل الطبيعي وهو الأول في أشرف المقدمتين وهي الصغرى على خلاف الثالث، فإنه يشارك الطبيعي في غيرها وهي الكبرى. ولأن ما ينتجه الثاني؛ وهو السالبة الكلية، فإنه أشرف ما ينتجه الثالث؛ وهو الموجبة الجزئية. ولا يقال كيف هو والكبرى أشرف من الصغرى لما أن القياس لا يتم إلا بالكبرى. وكذلك الموجبة الجزئية عن السالبة الكلية؛ إذ الموجبة تدل على وجود الشيء بخلاف السالبة. فإن القياس إنما يتم بالكبرى لكونها تالية للصغرى؛ وذلك يدل على تقدم الصغرى صورة ومعنى. ولأن الصغرى أشرف لكونها مشتملة على الأشرف وهو موضوع النتيجة. وقد علمت بأن الموضوع أشرف من المحمول. وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها وإن كانت تدل على وجود الشيء فلا تكون في قوة السالبة الكلية لما أن السالبة الكلية كالضد للموجبة الكلية، وضد الشيء في قوة ذلك الشيء. ولأنها منافية للموجبة الجزئية والكلية على خلاف الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تكون منافية للسالبة الجزئية والكلية، ولما كانت السالبة الكلية أقوى كانت أشرف وأفضل.
The second [figure] is determined as second because it shares with the natural figure—which is the first—the more honorable of the two premises, namely the minor, unlike the third, for it shares with the natural figure the other premise, namely the major. And because what the second produces—namely the universal negative—is more honorable than what the third produces—namely the particular affirmative. And it is not said, 'How can that be, while the major is more honorable than the minor, since the syllogism is not completed except by the major?' And likewise the particular affirmative [is not] about the universal negative; for the affirmative indicates the existence of a thing, unlike the negative. For the syllogism is only completed by the major because it follows the minor; that indicates the priority of the minor in form and meaning. And because the minor is more honorable for being inclusive of the more honorable, namely the subject of the conclusion. And you have known that the subject is more honorable than the predicate. As for the particular affirmative, although it indicates the existence of a thing, it is not equal in strength to the universal negative, because the universal negative is like the opposite of the universal affirmative, and the opposite of a thing is in the strength of that thing. And because it is contrary to the particular and universal affirmative, unlike the particular affirmative, for it is not contrary to the particular and universal negative. And since the universal negative is stronger, it is more honorable and superior.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis defending the superiority of the second figure over the third. The author begins by stating that the second figure shares the more honorable premise (the minor) with the first figure, whereas the third shares the less honorable premise (the major). He also argues that the conclusion of the second figure (universal negative) is more honorable than that of the third (particular affirmative) because the universal negative is equivalent in strength to the opposite of the universal affirmative, and the particular affirmative, though it indicates existence, does not have the same strength. The author addresses a potential objection: if the major premise is more honorable because the syllogism depends on it, why is the second figure superior? He replies that the minor premise is more honorable because it contains the subject, which is more honorable than the predicate. He then elaborates on the strength of the universal negative compared to the particular affirmative, noting that the universal negative is contrary to both universal and particular affirmatives, while the particular affirmative is not contrary to negatives. Therefore, the universal negative is stronger and more honorable. This argument is rooted in Aristotelian logic but uses honorific language typical of Islamic logical works.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
السالبة الكلية
الموجبة الجزئية

D) Because the universal negative is more commonly used in arguments.

Key Takeaway
In comparing syllogistic figures, the second figure is superior because it shares the minor premise with the first figure and yields a stronger (universal negative) conclusion.

Why the Third Figure is Closer to Nature
Author explains the third figure's virtue of proximity to nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the third and fourth figures in terms of their agreement with the first figure, and explain why the third is considered closer to nature.

Core Idea
The third figure shares one premise with the natural first figure; the fourth shares none.

In this passage, the author explains why the third figure is more natural than the fourth. He measures naturalness by agreement with the first figure (al-shakl al-tabi'i). Let's unpack this comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard of naturalness
The first figure is considered the natural figure (al-shakl al-tabi'i). The author uses it as the benchmark for measuring the naturalness of other figures.
The first figure is the most intuitive and self-evident syllogistic form.
2
Compare each figure's premises with the first
The third figure agrees with the first in one premise (either major or minor). The fourth figure disagrees in both premises. This makes the third closer to nature.
Agreement in one premise means the third figure retains some of the natural order.
3
Understand the consequence for understanding
The fourth figure is far from nature and requires more reflection and contemplation to clarify what follows. The third, though not self-evident, is still close enough that its syllogistic nature can be grasped without full analysis.
The author says one with innate intellectual faculty can see the third figure's conclusion before explicit proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما الثالث منها فإنه إنما يتعين لكونه تاليا ومقدما على الرابع [ث] لأنه يوافق الشكل الطبيعي في إحدى مقدميه بخلاف الرابع فإنه يخالف الطبيعي في كل واحدة من مقدّمتيه. ولهذا يكون بعيدًا عن الطبع ويحتاج في إبانة ما يلزم منه إلى زيادة تفكر وتأمل. والثالث وإن لم يكون لزوم ما يلزم عنه بينا بذاته فلا يكون بعيدا عن الطبع بل قريباً منه يمكن أن يفهم قياسيته قبل البيان لشيء آخر، من كان له قارحة وقارّة بين ذهنه إلى ما يلزم من غيره من غير أن ينظر فيما يبينه من الأمور ويقرره. ولهذا لم يتعرض السلف للشكل الرابع وإن كان هو من اللوازم في القسمة وإلا لا تكون القسمة عقلية بل يتعرضون للأشكال الثلاثة على الترتيب الذي مرَّ ذكره ومنهم من قال إنهم لم يتعرضوا له لكونه مفتقرا في البيان إلى أن يرد إلى الأول بطريق الانعكاس ليتبين به ما يلزم منه وهو عكس ما يلزم من الأول. ولو كان كذلك لكان التعرُّض للأول يكفي معونة التعرُّض له فيكون مفروعًا عنه إذن. غير أنه ضعيف جدا
[ت] As for the third [figure], it is determined as following and preceding the fourth because it agrees with the natural figure in one of its premises, unlike the fourth, for it disagrees with the natural figure in each of its two premises. Hence it is far from nature and requires an increase of reflection and contemplation to clarify what follows from it. The third, even if the inference of what follows from it is not self-evident, is not far from nature but rather close to it, such that its syllogistic nature can be understood before clarification by something else, for one who has an innate faculty and a settled mind to [infer] what follows from something else without considering the matters that clarify it and establish it. For this reason, the predecessors did not address the fourth figure, although it is among the necessary [divisions] in the classification; otherwise the classification would not be rational. Rather, they addressed the three figures in the order mentioned. And among them there is one who said that they did not address it because it requires, in clarification, to be reduced to the first by way of conversion to clarify what follows from it, and it is the inverse of what follows from the first. If that were the case, then addressing the first would suffice, with the help of addressing it, so it would be exhausted thereby. But that is very weak.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage discusses the position of the third and fourth figures. The author asserts that the third figure is closer to nature because it shares one premise with the first figure, whereas the fourth figure shares none, making it unnatural and requiring more effort. The third figure, though not self-evident, is still close to nature and can be understood by those with innate intellectual ability. The author then addresses why the predecessors (salaf) did not discuss the fourth figure. He mentions two opinions: first, that the fourth figure is a necessary division to make the classification rational; second, that they omitted it because it requires conversion to the first figure. The author dismisses the latter opinion as very weak.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
الطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It agrees in the conclusion.
D) It does not agree at all.

Key Takeaway
The third figure is closer to nature than the fourth because it shares one premise with the natural first figure, making its syllogistic form more intuitive.

Why Predecessors Neglected the Fourth Figure
Author explains reasons for predecessors' neglect of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two explanations given for the predecessors' neglect of the fourth figure and identify which the author considers weak.

Core Idea
Predecessors neglected the fourth figure because it is far from nature, not merely because it reduces to the first.

The author notes that the early scholars (salaf) did not discuss the fourth figure. He presents two possible reasons: its distance from nature, and the need to reduce it to the first figure via conversion. We'll examine his assessment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first reason for neglect
The author directly states 'ولهذا لم يتعرض السلف للشكل الرابع' (for this reason, the predecessors did not address the fourth figure), referring back to its distance from nature and need for more reflection.
The word 'ولهذا' (for this reason) connects the neglect to the preceding discussion about unnaturalness.
2
Consider the alternative explanation
Some said the predecessors omitted it because it requires reduction to the first figure by conversion, making it derivative. The author reports this view but dismisses it as 'very weak' (ضعيف جدا).
The reduction argument would imply that once the first figure is covered, the fourth is superfluous.
3
Assess the author's verdict
The author calls the reduction-based explanation very weak, implying that the primary reason is the figure's unnaturalness. However, he still notes that the fourth figure is a necessary division for a rational classification.
The author implies that simply covering the first figure does not suffice to cover the fourth, because the fourth has its own moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ت] As for the third [figure], it is determined as following and preceding the fourth because it agrees with the natural figure in one of its premises, unlike the fourth, for it disagrees with the natural figure in each of its two premises. Hence it is far from nature and requires an increase of reflection and contemplation to clarify what follows from it. The third, even if the inference of what follows from it is not self-evident, is not far from nature but rather close to it, such that its syllogistic nature can be understood before clarification by something else, for one who has an innate faculty and a settled mind to [infer] what follows from something else without considering the matters that clarify it and establish it. For this reason, the predecessors did not address the fourth figure, although it is among the necessary [divisions] in the classification; otherwise the classification would not be rational. Rather, they addressed the three figures in the order mentioned. And among them there is one who said that they did not address it because it requires, in clarification, to be reduced to the first by way of conversion to clarify what follows from it, and it is the inverse of what follows from the first. If that were the case, then addressing the first would suffice, with the help of addressing it, so it would be exhausted thereby. But that is very weak.

The author asserts that the third figure is closer to nature because it shares one premise with the first figure, whereas the fourth figure shares none, making it unnatural and requiring more effort. A) He accepts it as correct.
B) He calls it very weak.
C) He considers it the only reason.
D) He does not mention it.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure was neglected by early logicians primarily due to its distance from natural syllogistic order, not merely because of its reducibility to the first figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Some animals are not mammals; all mammals are warm-blooded; therefore some warm-blooded things are not animals.' Identify which figure this belongs to and discuss why it might be considered unnatural, based on the author's criteria.

Objection to the Division of Categorical Syllogisms
Author rebuts an objection on the division of syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid syllogistic compositions and those that include the conclusion explicitly, based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
A categorical syllogism must not contain the conclusion in its premises.

When studying the division of categorical syllogisms into four figures, one might object that certain combinations of premises are not covered. The author anticipates this and shows that such combinations are not truly syllogistic because they explicitly state the conclusion. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal, clarifying what qualifies as a categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's alternative combinations
The objector proposes combinations where the middle term is predicated of the middle in the minor, and the major is predicated of the minor in the major, or vice versa. These are not among the four figures.
These combinations include the conclusion explicitly stated.
2
Understand the author's definition of categorical syllogism
The author states that categorical syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-iqtiraniyya) are those where the conclusion is not explicitly mentioned in the premises.
If the conclusion appears in the premises, it is not a syllogism but a different form.
3
Apply the definition to the objection
The objector's combinations include the conclusion as a part of the premises, thus they do not qualify as categorical syllogisms.
4
Conclude that the division is exhaustive
Since only combinations without an explicit conclusion count, the four figures cover all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول ما ذكرتم من القسمة لا تكون قسمة عقلية فإن من الأقسام ما يكون خارجا عنها كما إذا كان الحد الأوسط محمولاً على الأوسط في الصغرى، والحد الأكبر محمولا على الأصغر في الكبرى، وبالعكس كذلك كما إذا كان الأكبر محمولاً على الأصغر في الصغرى، والأوسط على الأوسط في الكبرى. لكنا نقول: هذه القسمة بحسب التركيبات القياسية؛ وما ذكرتم من التركيبات لا يكون منها. ولأن الكلام في القياسات الاقترانية، والقياسات الاقترانية بمعزل عما تكون النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورًا فيه بالفعل؛ وما ذكرتم وإن كان من الأقسام العقلية فقد كان مشتملا على أحدهما وهو النتيجة؛ إذ هى مصرح بها فيه. فالاقترانية إذن منحصرة في الأقسام الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي الأشكال الأربعة . ثمَّ الثاني من الأشكال يرتد إلى الأول بعكس كبراه، والثالث منها يرتد إلى الأول بعكس صغراه، والرابع يرتد إليه بعكس صغراه وكبراه لما أنه يخالفه في كل واحدة منهما كما مر ؛ وهذه الأشكال الأربعة يشترك كل واحد منها في أنه [1] لا قياس عن جزئيتين أصلا، [ب] ولا عن سالبتين كذلك، [ت] ولا عن سالبة كبراها جزئية. وأما قولهم الا عن سالبتين ولا عن سالبة كبراها جزئية إلا في المواد الممكنة»، فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ السالبة من حيث هي السالبة لا تصلح
Then one may say: 'What you have mentioned of the division is not a rational classification, for there are divisions that fall outside it, such as when the middle term is predicated of the middle in the minor, and the major term is predicated of the minor in the major, and conversely also, such as when the major is predicated of the minor in the minor, and the middle is predicated of the middle in the major.' But we say: This division is according to syllogistic compositions; and the compositions you have mentioned are not among them. And because the discussion concerns categorical syllogisms, and categorical syllogisms are far from what [the conclusion] or its contradictory is actually mentioned in them; and what you have mentioned, although it is among the rational divisions, includes one of them, namely the conclusion, because it is explicitly stated in it. Therefore, categorical [syllogisms] are confined to the four divisions mentioned, namely the four figures. Then the second of the figures reduces to the first by converting its major; the third reduces to it by converting its minor; and the fourth reduces to it by converting its minor and major because it differs from it in each of them, as has passed. And these four figures share in that (1) there is no syllogism from two particulars at all, (2) nor from two negatives likewise, (3) nor from a negative whose major is particular. As for their saying: 'except from two negatives nor from a negative whose major is particular except in possible matters,' that is not as it should be, for the negative, in itself, is not suitable...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its response regarding the completeness of the division of categorical syllogisms into four figures. The objector presents two alternative combinations that are not covered. The author replies that these combinations are not syllogistic compositions because they involve the conclusion explicitly stated in the premises, which is not the nature of categorical syllogisms. Thus, the division is exhaustive. Then the author explains how each of the three non-first figures reduces to the first: the second by conversion of the major, the third by conversion of the minor, and the fourth by converting both. Finally, he states common conditions for a valid syllogism: no two particulars, no two negatives, and no negative major with a particular minor. He criticizes those who allow except in possible matters, because the negative qua negative is not suitable.

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism must not contain the conclusion in its premises; otherwise, it is not a true syllogism.

Reduction of Syllogistic Figures to the First
Author explains reduction of the second, third, and fourth figures to the first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion to reduce the second, third, and fourth figures to the first figure.

Core Idea
Every syllogism in figures 2-4 can be reduced to the first figure by converting one or both premises.

The first figure is the standard for evaluating syllogistic validity. The author explains how to transform syllogisms from other figures into the first figure using conversion (al-in'ikas). This lesson walks through the reduction method for each non-first figure, enabling you to test validity universally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine whether the syllogism is second, third, or fourth figure based on the position of the middle term.
Second: middle premise predicate and subject; Third: middle subject in both; Fourth: middle predicate in both.
2
Reduce second figure by converting the major premise
Take the major premise and swap its subject and predicate. This transforms the second figure into the first figure.
Standard conversion rules apply: E and I convert simply; A converts per accidens to I.
3
Reduce third figure by converting the minor premise
Take the minor premise and convert it. The resulting syllogism becomes first figure.
4
Reduce fourth figure by converting both premises
Convert both the major and minor premises. The resulting syllogism is first figure.
The conclusion may also need conversion to align with the first figure.
5
Verify validity in the first figure
Check that the reduced syllogism satisfies first figure conditions (e.g., mood constraints). If valid in first figure, the original is valid.

Common Conditions for Valid Categorical Syllogisms
Author states three common conditions valid for all four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given categorical syllogism violates any of the three common conditions: no two particulars, no two negatives, and no negative major that is particular.

Core Idea
A valid categorical syllogism must satisfy three simple conditions: at least one universal, at least one affirmative, and a universal major if the major is negative.

All four figures of categorical syllogisms share three conditions that must be met for validity. The author states them explicitly: no syllogism from two particular premises, no syllogism from two negative premises, and no syllogism where the major premise is negative and particular. These conditions are universal and help quickly eliminate many invalid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Check for two particular premises
If both premises are particular (i.e., I or O), the syllogism is invalid regardless of figure.
Example: 'Some A is B; some B is C' yields no necessary conclusion.
2
Check for two negative premises
If both premises are negative (E or O), no valid conclusion follows.
Negatives do not link the terms positively.
3
Check for a negative major that is particular
If the major premise is negative (E or O) and also particular (O), the syllogism is invalid.
The major premise must be universal if negative to distribute the middle term properly.
4
Apply the conditions to a sample syllogism
Take a given syllogism and test it against these three conditions. If any condition is violated, the syllogism is invalid.

Defending the Four Figures Against an Objection
Author defends the fourfold division of syllogistic figures against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection to the exhaustive division of syllogistic figures and explain the author's reply using the conditions of categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Only four arrangements of the middle term yield valid categorical syllogisms.

The logician sets out to prove that the four figures of categorical syllogisms are exhaustive. An objector presents alternative term orders that seem to be missing from the classification. The reply hinges on the definition of a categorical syllogism: the conclusion must not be stated as a premise. Let's follow the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says the fourfold division is not a rational division, because there are term orders that fall outside it. Look for the phrase 'فإن من الأقسام ما يكون خارجا عنها' (for there are divisions that fall outside it).
The objector gives two examples of alternative term arrangements.
2
Extract the objector's examples
First example: middle term predicated of middle in minor, major predicated of minor in major. Second example: major predicated of minor in minor, middle predicated of middle in major. Notice that both involve repeating the middle term in both premises.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author replies that his division is based on valid syllogistic compositions (تركيبات قياسية). The objector's examples are not such compositions. Why? Because they either contain the conclusion explicitly or have an invalid term structure. The key sentence: 'الكلام في القياسات الاقترانية، والقياسات الاقترانية بمعزل عما تكون النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورًا فيه بالفعل' (the discussion is about categorical syllogisms, and categorical syllogisms are remote from that in which the conclusion or its negation is stated explicitly).
The objector's examples include the conclusion as a premise, which violates the nature of a categorical syllogism where the conclusion is inferred.
4
Confirm the exhaustiveness
The author concludes that categorical syllogisms are confined to the four figures. That means any valid categorical syllogism must have one of the four term arrangements (middle subject in first, predicate in second, subject of major in third, etc.). The objection fails because it mistakenly counts non-standard term orders as categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول ما ذكرتم من القسمة لا تكون قسمة عقلية فإن من الأقسام ما يكون خارجا عنها كما إذا كان الحد الأوسط محمولاً على الأوسط في الصغرى، والحد الأكبر محمولا على الأصغر في الكبرى، وبالعكس كذلك كما إذا كان الأكبر محمولاً على الأصغر في الصغرى، والأوسط على الأوسط في الكبرى. لكنا نقول: هذه القسمة بحسب التركيبات القياسية؛ وما ذكرتم من التركيبات لا يكون منها. ولأن الكلام في القياسات الاقترانية، والقياسات الاقترانية بمعزل عما تكون النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورًا فيه بالفعل؛ وما ذكرتم وإن كان من الأقسام العقلية فقد كان مشتملا على أحدهما وهو النتيجة؛ إذ هى مصرح بها فيه. فالاقترانية إذن منحصرة في الأقسام الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي الأشكال الأربعة .
Then it may be said: What you have mentioned of the division is not a rational division, for there are divisions that fall outside it, such as when the middle term is predicated of the middle in the minor premise, and the major term is predicated of the minor in the major premise, and conversely as well, such as when the major is predicated of the minor in the minor premise, and the middle is predicated of the middle in the major premise. But we say: This division is according to syllogistic compositions; and what you have mentioned of compositions is not among them. And because the discussion is about categorical syllogisms, and categorical syllogisms are remote from that in which the conclusion or its negation is mentioned explicitly; and what you have mentioned, although it is among the rational divisions, includes one of them, namely the conclusion, for it is explicitly stated in it. Therefore categorical syllogisms are confined to the four divisions that have been mentioned, namely the four figures.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. The objector claims that the fourfold division of figures is not exhaustive, giving examples of alternative term orders: for instance, where the middle term is predicated of the middle in the minor (unusual), or where the major is predicated of the minor in the minor and the middle of the middle in the major. The author replies that the division is based on valid syllogistic composition (tarkibat qiyasiyya) and that the objector's examples are not valid categorical syllogisms because they either state the conclusion explicitly or involve term orders that are not standard for categorical syllogisms. In categorical syllogisms, the conclusion is not stated in the premises; it is inferred. Therefore, the objector's examples fall outside the scope of categorical syllogisms. Hence, the four figures (first, second, third, fourth) are exhaustive for all possible valid categorical syllogisms. The author thus defends the standard classification against a rational objection.

شكل

D) Because they are not in the first figure.

Key Takeaway
The four figures of categorical syllogisms are exhaustive because any valid categorical syllogism must have the conclusion inferred, not stated as a premise, and only four arrangements of the middle term satisfy this.

The Syllogism

Reduction of Figures and Common Conditions
Author explains reduction of second, third, fourth figures to first and states three universal conditions for valid syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any categorical syllogism in the second, third, or fourth figure to the first figure by converting the appropriate premises, and verify whether the syllogism violates the three common conditions (no two particulars, no two negatives, no particular negative major).

Core Idea
Every non-first figure syllogism can be reduced to the first by converting premises, but three universal conditions must first be satisfied.

In this passage, the author describes how the second, third, and fourth figures of categorical syllogisms are reduced to the first figure through a process called conversion (aks). He also states three universal conditions that all valid syllogisms must satisfy. Understanding these reductions and conditions will allow you to test the validity of any categorical syllogism by transforming it into the well-known first figure and checking for common fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the syllogism
Determine whether the syllogism is in the second, third, or fourth figure based on the position of the middle term. This passage tells you which conversion to apply for each figure.
The figures are defined by the arrangement of terms.
2
Determine which premises need conversion
For the second figure, convert the major premise only. For the third figure, convert the minor premise only. For the fourth figure, convert both premises. This is exactly what the passage states.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth (with the appropriate type of conversion).
3
Apply conversion correctly
Perform the conversion on the specified premise(s). Remember that universal affirmative (A) converts to particular affirmative (I) by accidental conversion; universal negative (E) converts to universal negative (E); particular affirmative (I) converts to particular affirmative (I); particular negative (O) does not convert.
Check the type of proposition (A, E, I, O) before converting.
4
Verify the three common conditions
After reduction, check that the syllogism does not have (1) two particular premises, (2) two negative premises, or (3) a negative major premise that is particular. If any of these conditions are violated, the syllogism is invalid regardless of figure.
These conditions ensure proper distribution of terms and a valid conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ الثاني من الأشكال يرتد إلى الأول بعكس كبراه، والثالث منها يرتد إلى الأول بعكس صغراه، والرابع يرتد إليه بعكس صغراه وكبراه لما أنه يخالفه في كل واحدة منهما كما مر ؛ وهذه الأشكال الأربعة يشترك كل واحد منها في أنه [1] لا قياس عن جزئيتين أصلا، [ب] ولا عن سالبتين كذلك، [ت] ولا عن سالبة كبراها جزئية.
Then the second of the figures reduces to the first by conversion of its major premise, and the third of them reduces to the first by conversion of its minor premise, and the fourth reduces to it by conversion of its minor and major premises because it differs from it in each of them as has passed. And these four figures share in that [1] there is no syllogism from two particulars at all, [b] nor from two negatives likewise, [t] nor from a negative major premise that is particular.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a concise explanation of how the second, third, and fourth figures are reduced to the first figure through conversion (aks) of premises. The second figure reduces by converting the major premise; the third by converting the minor; the fourth requires conversion of both. Then the author lists three general rules that apply to all four figures: no valid syllogism can have (1) two particular premises, (2) two negative premises, or (3) a major premise that is both negative and particular. These conditions ensure that the middle term is properly distributed and that a valid conclusion can be drawn. For instance, in a first figure syllogism like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden,' both premises are universal affirmative, satisfying the conditions.

Why Possible Negative Premises Do Not Excuse the Two-Negatives Rule
Author critiques the exception for possible negative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a negative premise is merely implied and explain why it cannot function as the true minor premise in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A negative premise implies an affirmative; the explicit negative is not the real minor.

In this passage, the author refines the rule that a syllogism cannot have two negatives or a particular negative major. Some logicians allow an exception when premises are about possible matters. The author argues that even in such cases, the negative premise is not the true minor—it implies an affirmative that must be the actual minor. Understanding this helps ensure that syllogistic conditions are applied correctly in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being discussed
Locate the general rule that a valid syllogism cannot have two negative premises or a negative major that is particular.
2
Find the claimed exception
Some logicians claim that in possible matters (materials that are possible, not necessary), this rule does not apply.
Possible matters: propositions with modality 'it is possible that...'
3
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that even in possible matters, the negative premise is not the real minor; it only implies an affirmative that is the true minor. The explicit negative is not the minor itself.
4
Test the implication
If the implied affirmative is not considered, no conclusion follows from the composite of premises. Therefore, the exception fails.
5
Connect to the conversion rule
The author ties this to an earlier rule that a possible negative does not convert, while a possible affirmative converts particularly. This asymmetry supports the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم الا عن سالبتين ولا عن سالبة كبراها جزئية إلا في المواد الممكنة»، فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ السالبة من حيث هي السالبة لا تصلح للصغروية إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة أو جزئية بل لا تصلح للصغروية في البعض من الأشكال البتة وأما في المواد الممكنة فإنها لا تكون هي الصغرى بنفسها بل هي مستلزمة لموجبة كانت تلك الموجبة هي الصغرى في الحقيقة حتى إذا لم تكن تلك الموجبة منظورًا إليها لم يلزم من ذلك المركب شيء ما البتة. ولما كانت الحالة هذه ؛ فالسالبة ليست هي الصغرى بعينها لا بالذات ولا بالعرض بل هي متضمنة للصغرى ومستلزمة لها. فلو كان المراد بالصغرى هو أن تكون مستلزمة لها فلا نزاع فيه ؛ وقد مرَّ من قبل في عكس السالبة الممكنة أنها لا تنعكس وإن كانت الموجبة الممكنة تنعكس عكسا جزئيا لما أن السالبة في نفسها غير الموجبة، والموجبة في نفسها غير السالبة.
As for their saying: 'except from two negatives and from a negative major premise that is particular except in possible matters,' that is not as it should be; for the negative insofar as it is negative is not suitable to be minor premise when the major is negative or particular; rather it is not suitable to be minor premise in some figures at all. As for possible matters, it is not the minor by itself, but it implies an affirmative that in reality is the minor, such that if that affirmative is not regarded, nothing follows from that composite at all. Since this is the case, the negative is not the minor itself, neither essentially nor accidentally, but it contains the minor and implies it. So if by 'minor' is meant that it implies it, then there is no dispute. And it has passed before concerning the conversion of the possible negative that it does not convert, even though the possible affirmative converts with a particular conversion, because the negative in itself is not the affirmative, and the affirmative in itself is not the negative.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the general rule that a valid syllogism cannot have two negatives or a particular negative major. Some logicians excepted cases where premises are about possible matters (e.g., 'it is possible that not P'). The author argues that even in such cases, the negative premise is not truly the minor premise; rather it implies an affirmative proposition that serves as the actual minor. For instance, 'It is possible that no intoxicant is haram' implies 'It is possible that some intoxicant is not haram' but that is still negative. The author insists the negative itself is not the real minor. The discussion ties back to the earlier rule on conversion of possible negatives: a possible negative does not convert (to a possible affirmative), whereas a possible affirmative converts particularly. This shows the asymmetry. Thus the purported exception is not valid because the negative premise only 'contains' the minor implicitly, and for a valid syllogism, the explicit premise must be the actual minor.

Key Terms
سالبة (saliba): A negative proposition (e.g., 'no S is P' or 'some S is not P'). [Note: Stable.]
مادة ممكنة (madda mumkina): A proposition with possible modality (e.g., 'it is possible that S is P'). [Note: In later modal logic, possible matter is treated differently; here it is about the content of the premise.]
صغرى (sughra): Minor premise; here the author distinguishes between the explicit premise and the implied one. [Note: No shift; same concept.]
مستلزمة (mustalzima): Implies; a negative premise implies an affirmative that functions as the real minor. [Note: Used in this specific context; not a standard term for implication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it cannot be converted.
B) Because it implies an affirmative that is the real minor.
C) Because it is particular.
D) Because it is negative.

Key Takeaway
A negative premise, even in possible matters, cannot serve as the true minor; it implies an affirmative that must be the actual minor for the syllogism to be valid.

Conclusion Follows the Inferior Premise
Author states rule that conclusion matches the inferior premise in quantity and quality, but not in modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the conclusion of a syllogism must match the inferior premise in quantity and quality, and identify the exception for modality.

Core Idea
The conclusion takes the weaker premise in quantity and quality.

In any valid categorical syllogism, the conclusion cannot be stronger than its premises. This passage from Mantiq Kabir establishes the key rule: the conclusion follows the 'inferior' (akhass) premise in both quantity (universal vs particular) and quality (affirmative vs negative). However, this rule does not apply to modality—that is a separate matter. Let's see how to apply this rule step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Write down the two premises of the syllogism. For example: P1: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative). P2: 'Some drink is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative).
Premises must share a middle term.
2
Assess inferiority in quantity
Check if any premise is particular (juz'iyya). The particular premise is the inferior in quantity; the conclusion must be particular if any premise is particular. If both are universal, the conclusion may be universal.
In the example, P2 is particular, so conclusion must be particular.
3
Assess inferiority in quality
Check if any premise is negative (saliba). The negative premise is the inferior in quality; the conclusion must be negative if any premise is negative. If both are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative.
In the example, both premises are affirmative, so conclusion is affirmative.
4
Combine and state the conclusion
The conclusion takes both inferiorities: from the example, it is particular affirmative: 'Some drink is forbidden.' If one premise were particular and the other negative, the conclusion would be particular negative.
The rule applies to quantity and quality simultaneously.
5
Remember the modality exception
The author states that modality (jiha) does not follow this rule—neither the inferior nor the superior premise determines the modality of the conclusion. That is covered in later sections.
Do not apply the 'inferior premise' rule to necessity, possibility, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النتيجة تتبع أخسَّ المقدمتين لا في كل شيء بل في الكمية والكيفية حتى إذا كانت إحدى مقدمتي القياس جزئيةً كانت النتيجة جزئية، وإذا كانت سالبة . كانت سالبة كذلك. وأما في الجهة فلا يتبع الأخس ولا غير الأخس أيضًا؛ [۱۳] وذلك يعرف من بعد فهذه هي أحكام كلية تعرفها بعد باعتبار / جزئياتها على سبيل التفصيل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises not in everything but in quantity and quality. Thus if one of the two premises of the syllogism is particular, the conclusion is particular; and if it is negative, the conclusion is negative likewise. As for modality, it does not follow the inferior nor the non-inferior either; [13] and that will be known later. So these are universal rules that will be known later by considering their particulars in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental principle of syllogistic logic: the conclusion must be in accordance with the 'inferior' (akhass) premise, meaning the premise that is more restricted in quantity or quality. If one premise is particular, the conclusion cannot be universal; if one premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative. For example, from 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative) and 'Some drink is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative), the conclusion must be particular: 'Some drink is forbidden.' Similarly, if one premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative. However, this rule does not apply to modality (jiha), such as necessity, possibility, or contingency. The author defers the detailed discussion of modality to a later section. This rule is crucial for determining the valid moods of each figure.

Key Terms
نتيجة (natija): the conclusion
أخس (akhass): the inferior premise
جهة (jiha): modality

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Quality
C) Modality
D) Both quantity and quality

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a syllogism must match the inferior premise in quantity and quality, but modality is a separate matter.

Why the First Figure Focuses on Four Quantified Propositions
Author introduces the first figure and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author restricts first-figure syllogisms to the four quantified propositions and identify the treatment of indefinite and particular propositions.

Core Idea
The first figure syllogisms need only the four quantified propositions; other types are redundant.

The first figure is the most important in categorical logic. The author tells us that the main goal is simple predicative syllogisms, and among these, only those made of the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are considered. Why? Because particular and indefinite propositions follow the rules of universals and particulars respectively, so covering them again would be repetitive. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Read the first sentence: the first figure is undoubtedly among the goals because the goals are simple syllogisms. The author is establishing the importance of the first figure.
2
Find the restriction to four quantified propositions
The author says 'what is considered from these syllogisms is what is composed of the four quantified propositions'. That means only syllogisms with universal or particular, affirmative or negative premises are accepted.
3
Understand the justification for excluding other propositions
The author argues that particulars follow rules of universals, and indefinites follow rules of particulars. Since these were already covered, repeating them would be against wisdom.
The 'muhmalat' (indefinite) are like 'man is mortal' without quantifier; they are treated as particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الشكل الأول ] أما الشكل الأول فإنه من المقاصد لا محالة لما أن المقاصد فيما نحن فيه هي الأقيسة البسيطة من الحمليّات. والمعتبر من هذه الأقيسة ما يتركب من المحصورات الأربع؛ إذ المخصوصات أحكامها أحكام الكليات والمهملات أحكامها أحكام الجزئيات. فبعد التعرض للكليات والجزئيات كان التعرض لكل واحدة منهما مشتملا على التكرار ؛ وذلك لا يكون على وفق الحكمة.
Section: On the First Figure. As for the first figure, it is undoubtedly among the goals, because the goals here are simple syllogisms from predicative propositions. And what is considered from these syllogisms is what is composed of the four quantified propositions; for the particulars follow the rules of universals and the indefinite follow the rules of particulars. So after dealing with universals and particulars, dealing with each of them would involve repetition; and that is not in accordance with wisdom.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the first figure, which is the most important figure in Aristotelian logic. The author states that the main purpose (maqasid) is simple categorical syllogisms (qiyas basit hamli), and among these, only those composed of the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are considered. This is because particular propositions (al-mukhasasat) are treated under the rules of universals, and indefinite propositions (al-muhmalat) are treated under the rules of particulars. Therefore, having already discussed universals and particulars elsewhere, discussing them again here would be redundant. The author thus justifies focusing directly on the first figure syllogisms composed of quantified propositions without repeating earlier material.

The first figure syllogisms are built from the four quantified propositions because other proposition types are already covered by earlier discussions, avoiding repetition.

The Syllogism

8 min
Conditions for First Figure Productivity
Author explains conditions for first figure productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a first figure categorical syllogism is valid by checking that the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise is universal.

Core Idea
First figure yields a conclusion only if the minor is affirmative and the major is universal.

The first figure is the most powerful syllogistic form, but not every pair of premises produces a conclusion. The author lays down two precise conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid proofs in Kalam and Fiqh. Let's unpack them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
In any valid first figure syllogism, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. Locate the two premises in the argument and label them as minor (containing the subject of the conclusion) and major (containing the predicate of the conclusion).
2
Check the minor premise's quality
The condition is that the minor must be affirmative. Ask: Is the minor premise a positive statement? If it is negative, then the middle term is denied of the minor term, breaking the subsumption chain necessary for the conclusion. If it is affirmative, proceed.
3
Check the major premise's quantity
The major must be universal (either affirmative or negative). Ask: Does the major premise begin with 'every' or 'no'? If it is particular (e.g., 'some'), the middle term may be only partially connected to the major term, and the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
4
Apply the conditions to a concrete example
Take a valid Islamic syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (major), 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor). Minor is affirmative, major is universal → valid. Now contrast with an invalid one: 'Some intoxicant is haram' (major is particular) → check if a conclusion follows? The author says no, because the major's partiality breaks certainty.
5
Synthesize the rationale
The author provides a logical reason for each condition. For the minor: if negative, the minor term does not fall under the middle. For the major: if particular, the major may not apply to the whole middle, so it may not extend to the minor. Make sure you can reproduce the reasoning in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الشكل الأول ]
أما الشكل الأول فإنه من المقاصد لا محالة لما أن المقاصد فيما نحن فيه هي الأقيسة البسيطة من الحمليّات. والمعتبر من هذه الأقيسة ما يتركب من المحصورات الأربع؛ إذ المخصوصات أحكامها أحكام الكليات والمهملات أحكامها أحكام الجزئيات. فبعد التعرض للكليات والجزئيات كان التعرض لكل واحدة منهما مشتملا على التكرار ؛ وذلك لا يكون على وفق الحكمة.
ثم المهملة إذا كانت إحدى مقدمتي القياس كانت هي الصغرى في هذا الشكل لا محالة. فإن من الشرائط أن تكون الكبرى كلية فلو كانت الصغرى مهملة كانت النتيجة مهملة كذلك لما مر من قبل أن النتيجة تتبع أخس المقدمتين فيقال مثلا «أب» وكل «ب ج» فينتج «أج». وأما المخصوصة فإنها قد تكون هي الصغرى وقد تكون هي الكبرى كذلك لكن لا فائدة في إقامة الأقيسة عليها، وإن كانت فهي قليلة أيضًا. فإن في الأكثر من الصور تكون خالية عنها كما إذا قلت هذا زيد، وزيد أبو عبد الله فهذا أبو عبد الله لم يكن علمك بأن هذا أبو عبد الله علما لا يحصل إلا بهذا التركيب والترتيب؛ فإن من كان بينا له أن هذا أبو عبد الله وهذا هو بعينه زيد كان بينا له أن زيدًا أبو عبد الله . ولما خرجت المخصوصات والمهملات عن البين بقيت المركبات الحاصلة من المحصورات وهي أربع موجبة كلية، وموجبة جزئية، وسالبة كلية، وسالبة جزئية؛ فيحصل منها ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة إذن وهي ستة عشر لكن البعض منها ساقط لما أن الشرط في إنتاج هذا الشكل هو أن تكون الصغرى موجبة والكبرى كلية أما كون الصغرى موجبة فلأنها إذا لم تكن موجبة فلا يندرج الأصغر تحت الأوسط؛ ولزوم النتيجة في هذا الشكل باندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط وباندراج الأوسط تحت الأكبر فإلّا لم يندرج الأصغر تحت الأوسط فلا يمكن أن يتعدى الأكبر إلى الأصغر. والصغرى إذا كانت سالبة كان الأوسط مسلوبًا عن الأصغر. ولو كان مسلوبًا عنه فلا يكون محمولاً عليه فلا يكون مندرجا تحته أعنى تحت الأوسط. وأما كون الكبرى كلية فلأنها إذا كانت جزئية فلا يلزم أن يكون الذي حكم به على الأصغر هو الذي حكم عليه بالأكبر. فإن من الممكن أن يكون الأوسط أعم من الأصغر وكان الأكبر مقولا على البعض ليس بأصغر. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت
Section: On the First Figure. As for the first figure, it is among the purposes undoubtedly, because the purposes in our subject are the simple syllogisms composed of categorical [propositions]. And what is considered from these syllogisms is what is composed of the four quantified [propositions]; for the designated [propositions] have the same rulings as universals, and the indefinite [propositions] have the same rulings as particulars. So after dealing with universals and particulars, dealing with each one of them would involve repetition; and that would not be in accordance with wisdom. Then the indefinite [proposition], if it is one of the two premises of a syllogism, is necessarily the minor [premise] in this figure. For it is among the conditions that the major be universal. So if the minor is indefinite, the conclusion would be indefinite likewise, due to what has passed that the conclusion follows the weaker of the two premises. For example, one says 'A is B' and 'Every B is C', so it yields 'A is C'. As for the designated [proposition], it may be the minor or it may be the major likewise, but there is no benefit in constructing syllogisms with them; and if there are, they are few. For in most cases, they are free from them, as when you say 'This is Zayd' and 'Zayd is Abu Abdullah', then 'This is Abu Abdullah'—your knowledge that this is Abu Abdullah is not knowledge that is not obtained except through this composition and arrangement; for he to whom it is evident that this is Abu Abdullah and that this is exactly Zayd, it is evident to him that Zayd is Abu Abdullah. And when the designated and indefinite [propositions] are excluded from consideration, what remains are the composites obtained from the four quantified [propositions], and they are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. From them, there results what results from multiplying four by four, i.e., sixteen, but some of them drop out, because the condition for productivity in this figure is that the minor be affirmative and the major be universal. As for the minor being affirmative, because if it is not affirmative, then the minor term does not fall under the middle term; and the necessity of the conclusion in this figure is due to the minor term falling under the middle term and the middle term falling under the major term. If the minor term does not fall under the middle term, it is impossible for the major term to extend to the minor term. And if the minor is negative, then the middle term is negated from the minor term. And if it is negated from it, then it is not predicated of it, so it is not falling under it—I mean under the middle term. As for the major being universal, because if it is particular, it does not necessarily follow that what is judged of the minor term is that which is judged of the major term. For it is possible that the middle term is more general than the minor term and the major term is said of some [of the middle] not of the minor. Do you not see that if you say...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author explains that the first figure is the primary aim in logic because it yields simple syllogisms. He first clarifies that only syllogisms composed of quantified propositions (universal or particular) are considered, because designated (singular) propositions follow universal rules and indefinite (unquantified) propositions follow particular rules—thus including them would be redundant. He then addresses indefinite and designated premises in the first figure: an indefinite premise must be the minor premise and is treated as particular; the conclusion will also be indefinite. Designated premises can be either minor or major but are useless for constructing syllogisms because the conclusion is already known directly (e.g., 'This is Zayd, Zayd is Abu Abdullah, so this is Abu Abdullah' is obvious without syllogistic form). Therefore, only the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) are used. Multiplying them yields sixteen possible combinations of two premises. However, many are invalid. The two essential conditions for a productive first figure are: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) the major premise must be universal. He proves each: (i) If the minor is negative, the middle term is denied of the minor, so the minor does not fall under the middle, and consequently the major cannot be extended to the minor—no conclusion follows. (ii) If the major is particular, the middle may be more general than the minor and the major said of only part of the middle, so the major may not apply to the minor. The text ends mid-example, but the point is clear.

الكبرى

Checkpoint
Why must the minor premise be affirmative in the first figure?
A) Because a negative minor would make the middle term negated of the minor, preventing the minor from falling under the middle, thus the major cannot apply to the minor.
B) Because if the minor is negative, the conclusion would also be negative, weakening the argument.
C) Because the first figure requires both premises to be affirmative.
D) Because a negative minor would make the major premise redundant.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's two inviolable conditions—minor affirmative and major universal—guarantee that the minor term falls under the middle and the middle under the major, ensuring a necessary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
