The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions of the First Figure and Its Four Valid Moods
Author derives conditions and lists four valid moods of first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism and enumerate the four productive moods with their premise types and conclusions.

Core Idea
The first figure requires a minor that is affirmative and a major that is universal; only four moods survive.

In this passage, the author explains why indefinite and particular premises are excluded, then derives the two necessary conditions for a productive syllogism in the first figure. He then lists the four valid moods (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio) using letter variables. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating any categorical syllogism in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and locate where the author states that the minor must be affirmative (صغرى موجبة) and the major must be universal (كبرى كلية). These are the two indispensable conditions for a productive first-figure syllogism.
The author calls them شرائط الإنتاج (conditions of productivity).
2
Understand why the minor must be affirmative
The author argues that in the first figure, the minor term must fall under the middle term (اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط). If the minor premise is negative, the middle is denied of the minor, so subsumption fails, and the conclusion cannot be drawn. Trace this reasoning in the text.
Counterexample given: a negative minor would not allow the middle to be predicated of the minor.
3
Understand why the major must be universal
The author argues that if the major premise is particular, the middle term might not be connected to all instances of the major, so the conclusion may not follow. He gives two counterexamples: (1) 'No human is a horse, every horse is an animal' does not prove 'No human is an animal'; (2) 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' does not prove 'Some human is a horse'.
The major must be universal to ensure the middle's relation to the major applies to every instance covered by the minor.
4
Eliminate invalid combinations
Starting from 16 possible combinations of four minor types (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) and four major types, apply the two conditions. Discard all with a negative minor (8 combinations) and all with a particular major (4 more). Verify that only four moods remain.
The author explicitly counts: 16 total, subtract 8 (negative minors), subtract 4 (particular majors), leaving 4.
5
Memorise the four valid moods
The author lists the four moods with their premise types and conclusions using letters (ج, ب, أ). Identify each mood: (1) both universal affirmative (Barbara); (2) universal affirmative minor, universal negative major (Celarent); (3) particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major (Darii); (4) particular affirmative minor, universal negative major (Ferio).
The first mood is the most perfect because it yields a universal affirmative conclusion.

[أ] أما الضرب الأول فالأوسط فيه قد يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له. وكذلك بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فإنه قد يكون أخص بالنسبة إلى الأكبر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا، فلا جرم يقع
Then the indefinite [premise], if it is one of the two premises of the syllogism, is necessarily the minor in this figure. For among the conditions is that the major be universal; so if the minor were indefinite, the conclusion would be indefinite as well, as previously mentioned that the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises. So it is said, for example, 'A B' and 'Every B C' thus produces 'A C'. As for the particular [premise], it may be the minor and may also be the major, but there is no utility in constructing syllogisms from them, and even if there is, it is rare. For in most of the forms they are absent, as when you say 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is Abu Abdullah', so 'This is Abu Abdullah' – your knowledge that this is Abu Abdullah is not knowledge that is obtained except through this composition and arrangement. For he for whom it is evident that this is Abu Abdullah and that this is the very same Zayd, it is evident to him that Zayd is Abu Abdullah. When the particulars and indefinites were excluded from consideration, there remained the compounds formed from the quantifiers, which are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. So from them there results what results from multiplying four by four, i.e., sixteen, but some of them fall away because the condition for productivity in this figure is that the minor be affirmative and the major be universal. As for the minor being affirmative, because if it is not affirmative, then the minor term does not fall under the middle term; and the necessity of the conclusion in this figure is by the falling of the minor under the middle and the falling of the middle under the major. So if the minor does not fall under the middle, it is impossible for the major to extend to the minor. And if the minor is negative, the middle is denied of the minor. And if it is denied of it, then it is not predicated of it, so it does not fall under it, i.e., under the middle. As for the major being universal, because if it is particular, it is not necessary that that which is judged of the minor be the same as that which is judged of the major. For it is possible that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is said of some but not of the minor. Do you not see that if you say 'No human is a horse, and every horse is an animal', it does not produce 'No human is an animal'. Similarly, if you say 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse', it does not produce 'Some human is a horse'. And if there were no condition of the affirmation of the minor and universality of the major as mentioned, this would necessarily be productive; and that is so. Since the negative is excluded and has no consideration in the minor whether universal or particular, there fall away from the sixteen moods eight: four with the universal negative in the minor, and four with the particular negative likewise. Similarly, the particular, if it is excluded and has no consideration in the major whether affirmative or negative, there fall away from the remaining moods another four: two with the universal affirmative in the minor, and two with the particular affirmative likewise. So there remain four moods, and they alone are productive in this figure. [1] The first mood: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a universal affirmative, thus: Every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A. [B] The second: from two universals with the major negative, it produces a universal negative, thus: Every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A. [T] The third: from two affirmatives with the minor particular, it produces a particular affirmative: Some J is B, and every B is A, so some J is A. [Th] The fourth: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, it produces a particular negative: Some J is B, and no B is A, so some J is not A. Then each of these four moods occurs in different manners according to the differences in their terms regarding generality, specificity, equality, and what is not one of them. [A] As for the first mood, the middle term therein may be more general with respect to the minor, or it may not but rather be equal to it. Similarly, with respect to the major, it may be more specific with respect to the major, or it may not but rather be equal. So it occurs indeed...
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the conditions for valid syllogisms in the first figure and the resulting four productive moods. The author begins by discussing indefinite (مهملة) and particular (مخصوصة) premises. An indefinite premise, if used, must be the minor premise because the major must be universal; otherwise the conclusion would be indefinite, following the principle that the conclusion follows the inferior premise. Particular premises are possible but rarely useful because they often lead to conclusions already known, as illustrated by the example of Zayd and Abu Abdullah. After excluding these, the author considers only quantified premises (محصورات): universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. Multiplying four types of minor and four types of major yields sixteen combinations, but many are invalid due to two conditions: (1) the minor must be affirmative, and (2) the major must be universal. The reason for the minor being affirmative is that the minor term must fall under the middle term for the conclusion to be necessary; a negative minor would deny this subsumption. The reason for the major being universal is that a particular major would not guarantee that what is true of the middle is true of all of the minor; a counterexample is given: 'No human is a horse, every horse is an animal' does not prove 'No human is an animal', and 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' does not prove 'Some human is a horse'. Thus, eight combinations with a negative minor are excluded, and four more with a particular major are excluded, leaving only four valid moods. These are enumerated with the notation using letters ج (J), ب (B), أ (A). The first mood (Barbara): universal affirmative premises yield universal affirmative conclusion. The second (Celarent): universal affirmative minor, universal negative major yield universal negative conclusion. The third (Darii): particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major yield particular affirmative conclusion. The fourth (Ferio): particular affirmative minor, universal negative major yield particular negative conclusion. Finally, the author notes that within each mood, the relative scope of terms (generality, equality, etc.) can vary, especially in the first mood where the middle may be more general or equal relative to the minor and major. This passage is foundational for understanding the valid forms of the first figure, which is considered the most perfect syllogistic figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author explains why eight moods are invalid and lists the four productive moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure and eliminate invalid premise combinations based on the rules that the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal.

Core Idea
Only four of sixteen premise pairs yield a conclusion: all have an affirmative minor and a universal major.

In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, the positions of the middle term enforce strict conditions on the premises. The author of this passage explains why only four out of sixteen possible mood-mood combinations are valid, and lists them with clear examples. By understanding this elimination, you will see how logical rules prune away invalid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rules of the first figure
The middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. The minor premise must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. These are derived from the categorical rules.
2
Eliminate moods with a negative minor
Since the minor must be affirmative, all eight moods where the minor is negative (whether universal or particular) are invalid. That leaves eight moods with an affirmative minor.
This is the first elimination step in the passage.
3
Eliminate moods with a particular major
Among the remaining eight, the major premise must be universal. So eliminate the four moods that have a particular major (whether affirmative or negative). That leaves four moods.
These four have an affirmative minor and a universal major.
4
Identify the four productive moods
The remaining four differ by quantity and quality of the minor and major premises: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major -> universal affirmative conclusion (Barbara); (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major -> universal negative conclusion (Celarent); (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major -> particular affirmative conclusion (Darii); (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major -> particular negative conclusion (Ferio).
These correspond to the four moods listed in the passage using Arabic variables ج, ب, أ.
5
Verify with an Islamic example
Take Darii: 'Some animal is a horse; every horse is mortal; therefore some animal is mortal.' This matches the third mood in the passage.
Check that the middle term (horse) fulfills the first-figure conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت السالبة ساقطة لا اعتبار لها في الصغرى كلية كانت أو جزئية فقد سقطت من الضروب الستة عشر ؛ ثمانية: أربعة مع السالبة الكلية في الصغرى، وأربعة مع السالبة الجزئية كذلك. وكذلك الجزئية إذا كانت ساقطة لا اعتبار لها في الكبرى موجبة كانت أو سالبة فقد سقطت من الضروب الباقية أربعة أخرى اثنان منها مع الموجبة الكلية في الصغرى، واثنان مع الموجبة الجزئية كذلك؛ فبقيت الأربعة من الضروب وإنها هي المنتجة في هذا الشكل لا غير. [1] فالضرب الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة كلية هكذا كل «ج» «ب»، وكل «ب أ» فكل «ج» «أ»؛ [ب] والثاني من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة كلية هكذا كل «ج» «ب»، ولا شيء من ب أ) فلا شيء من «ج» «أ»؛ [ت] والثالث من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية، بعض «ج» «ب»، وكل ب أ ) فبعض «ج» «أ»؛ [ث] والرابع من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، بعض «ج» «ب»، ولا شيء من «ب أ» فبعض «ج» ليس «أ».
And since the negative is invalid and has no consideration in the minor premise, whether universal or particular, then eight of the sixteen moods drop: four with the universal negative in the minor, and four with the particular negative likewise. And likewise, the particular [premise] when it is invalid and has no consideration in the major premise, whether affirmative or negative, then from the remaining moods another four drop: two of them with the universal affirmative in the minor, and two with the particular affirmative likewise; thus the four moods remain, and they are the only productive ones in this figure. [1] So the first mood from two universal affirmatives produces a universal affirmative, thus: every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A; [B] The second from two universals and the major negative produces a universal negative, thus: every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A; [T] The third from two affirmatives and the minor particular produces a particular affirmative: some J is B, and every B is A, so some J is A; [Th] The fourth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative: some J is B, and no B is A, so some J is not A.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage classifies the valid moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by explaining why only four of the sixteen possible combinations of premises are productive. The key conditions are: the minor premise must be affirmative (cannot be negative), and the major premise must be universal (cannot be particular). This is derived from earlier rules. Thus, eight moods are eliminated because they have a negative minor (whether universal or particular). Then, from the remaining eight that have an affirmative minor, another four are eliminated because they have a particular major (whether affirmative or negative). Only four moods remain, each with a specific combination of quantity and quality. The first mood (AAA-1) has two universal affirmatives and produces a universal affirmative conclusion. The second (EAE-1) has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, producing a universal negative. The third (AII-1) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, producing a particular affirmative. The fourth (EIO-1) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, producing a particular negative. The author uses the letters ج (the minor term), ب (the middle term), and أ (the major term) as variables. For example, in the first mood: 'Every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A.' This corresponds to the standard first-figure moods: Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio in Latin mnemonic. The passage is an expansion of the matn that states these moods.

Key Terms
الصغرى (al-sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the minor term (subject of the conclusion) and the middle term. [Note: No significant shift.]
الكبرى (al-kubra): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the major term (predicate of the conclusion) and the middle term. [Note: No significant shift.]
ساقطة (saqita): Invalid or discarded; here, a premise combination that does not yield a conclusion.
الضرب (al-darb): A mood of a syllogism, determined by the quantity and quality of the premises. [Note: In later texts, darb can also refer to a syllogistic form, but here it is specifically the mood.]
منتجة (muntija): Productive; yielding a valid conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every intoxicant is forbidden; some drink is intoxicant; therefore some drink is forbidden.
B) Every Muslim is human; no human is immortal; therefore every Muslim is not immortal.
C) Some animal is horse; every horse is mortal; therefore some animal is mortal.
D) No wine is halal; every nabidh is wine; therefore no nabidh is halal.

Key Takeaway
The first figure has four valid moods, all with an affirmative minor and a universal major, yielding conclusions that are either universal or particular, affirmative or negative.

The Syllogism

Variants of Each Mood Based on Relative Generality
Author analyzes the possible relations of the middle term to the extremes in each mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the possible relations of the middle term to the extremes for each mood of the first figure and calculate the number of variants for each mood.

Core Idea
A single mood can have multiple variants depending on how the middle term relates in generality to the minor and major terms.

In the previous lesson, we learned the four productive moods of the first figure. However, each mood can occur in different ways depending on the relative generality of the middle term compared to the minor and major terms. In this passage, the author systematically explores these variants, showing that the first mood has four, the second has two, the third has eight, and the fourth has four variants. Understanding these variants is crucial for correctly applying syllogisms in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four possible relations between two terms
The author considers four ways a term can relate to another in extension: more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), equal (musawi), or none of these (i.e., disparate/mubayana). This classification allows for a systematic analysis of the middle term's relation to each extreme.
The relation 'none' means the two terms have no overlap in extension, which is required for negative premises.
2
Analyze the first mood (AAA-1)
For the first mood (universal affirmative minor and major), the middle term relative to the minor can be more general or equal (2 possibilities). Relative to the major, it can be more specific or equal (2 possibilities). Thus, 2 × 2 = 4 variants for this mood.
The extremes themselves do not directly relate; only the middle's relation to each matters.
3
Analyze the second mood (EAE-1)
For the second mood (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major), the middle relative to the minor can be more general or equal (2 possibilities). Relative to the major, it must be incompatible (mubayana) for the negative premise to hold, so only 1 possibility. Hence, 2 × 1 = 2 variants.
The incompatibility ensures the negative premise is true.
4
Analyze the third mood (AII-1)
For the third mood (particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major), the middle relative to the minor can be any of the four (more general, more specific, equal, or none). Relative to the major, it can be more specific or equal (2 possibilities). Thus, 4 × 2 = 8 variants.
The particular minor allows for more flexibility.
5
Analyze the fourth mood (EIO-1)
For the fourth mood (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), the middle relative to the minor can be any of the four (as in the third mood). Relative to the major, it must be incompatible (mubayana) for the negative premise, so only 1 possibility. Hence, 4 × 1 = 4 variants.
The incompatibility constraint reduces the number of variants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل واحد من هذه الضروب الأربعة يقع على وجوه مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الأحوال في حدودها من العموم والخصوص والمساواة وما ليس بواحد منها . [أ] أما الضرب الأول فالأوسط فيه قد يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له. وكذلك بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فإنه قد يكون أخص بالنسبة إلى الأكبر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا، فلا جرم يقع على أربعة أوجه بهذه النسبة، وفي نسبة الأوسط إلى كل واحد من الطرفين لا بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين إلى صاحبه. [ب] وأما الضرب الثاني فالأوسط فيه قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الأكبر أو مساويًا، وقد كان بينه وبين الأكبر مباينة بالضرورة وإلا لا يمكن أن يسلب عنه، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يقع الا على وجهين، وهذا بالنسبة التي مر ذكرها وهي نسبة الأوسط إلى الطرفين (۱). [ت] وأما الضرب الثالث فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه ، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويًا ولا أخص منه ولا أعم كذلك. وأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فإما أن يكون أخص منه، أو مساويًا. فلا جرم يقع هذا الضرب على ثمانية أوجه؛ وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الاثنين من جانب الأكبر، وهذا بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لا بالنسبة بين الطرفين. [ث] وأما الضرب الرابع فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويا له ولا أخص منه ولا أعم أيضًا كما في الثالث. فأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فلا يمكن أن يكون أخص منه ولا أعم ولا غير الأعم من الأربعة المذكورة ضرورة / يتحقق المباينة بينه وبين الأكبر على ما عرف. ولو كان كذلك فلا [١٥٤ و] يمكن أن يقع هذا الضرب بتلك النسبة إلا على أربعة أوجه وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الواحد من جانب الأكبر: فهذه
Then each one of these four moods occurs in different ways according to the difference of states in their terms of generality, specificity, equality, and what is not one of them. [A] As for the first mood, the middle term in it may be more general relative to the minor, or it may not be but rather equal to it. And likewise relative to the major, it may be more specific relative to the major, or it may not be but rather equal to it. Hence it occurs in four ways according to this relation, and in the relation of the middle to each of the two extremes, not in the relation of each of the two extremes to each other. [B] As for the second mood, the middle term in it may be more general than the minor, or it may not be but rather equal to it; and it cannot be more general than the major or equal to it, for there is necessarily a separation between it and the major, otherwise it could not be negated from it. Therefore it cannot occur except in two ways, and this is according to the relation mentioned, which is the relation of the middle to the two extremes. [T] As for the third mood, the middle term in it relative to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be but rather equal to it, or it is neither equal nor more specific nor more general likewise. And relative to the major, it is either more specific than it or equal to it. Hence this mood occurs in eight ways; which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the two from the side of the major, and this is according to the relation mentioned, not the relation between the two extremes. [Th] As for the fourth mood, the middle term in it relative to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be but rather equal to it, or it is neither equal to it nor more specific nor more general also, as in the third. As for relative to the major, it cannot be more specific than it nor more general nor other than the four mentioned, because necessarily the separation between it and the major is realized, as known. Therefore, this mood cannot occur in that relation except in four ways, which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the one from the side of the major: so these are...
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the possible relations of the middle term to the minor and major terms in each of the four productive moods of the first figure. The author considers four possible relations: the middle can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), equal (musawi), or none of these (ma laysa bi-wahid minha). However, not all combinations are possible due to logical constraints. For the first mood (AAA-1 with universal affirmative premises), the middle term relative to the minor may be more general or equal, and relative to the major may be more specific or equal. This gives 2 × 2 = 4 possible variants. The relation between the extremes themselves is not considered. For the second mood (EAE-1 with universal affirmative minor and universal negative major), the middle relative to the minor may be more general or equal, but relative to the major it cannot be more general or equal because the major term must be negated of the middle; thus the middle must be incompatible (mubayana) with the major. Therefore, there is only one possibility relative to the major (the relation is fixed as 'not more general or equal'), but the author says it occurs in two ways, implying that the relation to the minor gives two (more general or equal), and the relation to the major is forced, so total = 2 × 1 = 2. For the third mood (AII-1 with particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major), the middle relative to the minor can be any of the four (more general, more specific, equal, or none), and relative to the major it must be more specific or equal (since the major is affirmed of the middle but not necessarily universal). So 4 × 2 = 8 ways. For the fourth mood (EIO-1 with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major), the middle relative to the minor can be the same four as in the third, but relative to the major it must be incompatible (mubayana) — which effectively means it cannot be more specific, more general, or any of the four categories? Wait, the author says 'it cannot be more specific than it nor more general nor other than the four mentioned' — this is tricky. He means that the only possible relation to the major is a fixed one: since the major is universally denied of the middle, the middle must be separate from the major in extension, i.e., there is no overlap. That does not fit into the four categories of generality, specificity, equality, or 'none'? Actually 'none' means none of the three (i.e., it is not related by generality, specificity, or equality, which for two terms means they are disparate). That's exactly the case here. So the only possibility relative to the major is that the middle is 'none of the three' (i.e., mubayana). But the author counts that as one possibility. So from the side of the major, there is 1 'way' (the forced mubayana). Thus 4 × 1 = 4 ways. The passage concludes by stating these numbers, showing the richness of syllogistic forms even within the same mood. This analysis is important for understanding the different ways a syllogism can be instantiated in natural language or in scientific demonstration.

Key Takeaway
The middle term's relation to the extremes determines the number of ways a mood can be instantiated, which is essential for precise syllogistic analysis.

?

Second Figure: Constraints on the Middle Term
Author describes the second figure constraints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the constraints on the middle term's relation to the extremes in the second figure and compute its valid faces.

Core Idea
Second figure forces the middle term to be more general than the minor and disparate from the major.

The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate of both premises. This imposes specific restrictions on the logical relations between the middle and the extremes. In this lesson, we will walk through the constraints and see why only two valid faces are possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the middle term in the second figure
Recall that in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. This position determines the possible relations.
The middle is predicate in both major and minor premises.
2
Determine possible relations between middle and minor
The author says the middle may be more general than the minor, or equal to it. It cannot be more specific or disparate.
Because if the middle is subject of the minor? Actually, think: if the minor is subject of the conclusion, its predicate in the premise is the middle. For the syllogism to be valid, the middle must be at least as general as the minor.
3
Determine possible relations between middle and major
The middle cannot be more general than or equal to the major; it must be disparate from the major.
Because if the middle were more general than the major, the major would be subordinate, leading to invalid conclusions. Disparity is necessary for a negative conclusion.
4
Combine to find the number of faces
The middle has 2 possible relations to the minor (more general or equal) and 1 fixed relation to the major (disparate). This yields 2 valid faces (2 × 1 = 2).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أربعة أوجه بهذه النسبة، وفي نسبة الأوسط إلى كل واحد من الطرفين لا بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين إلى صاحبه.
[ب] وأما الضرب الثاني فالأوسط فيه قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الأكبر أو مساويًا، وقد كان بينه وبين الأكبر مباينة بالضرورة وإلا لا يمكن أن يسلب عنه، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يقع الا على وجهين، وهذا بالنسبة التي مر ذكرها وهي نسبة الأوسط إلى الطرفين (۱).
[ت] وأما الضرب الثالث فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه ، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويًا ولا أخص منه ولا أعم كذلك. وأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فإما أن يكون أخص منه، أو مساويًا. فلا جرم يقع هذا الضرب على ثمانية أوجه؛ وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الاثنين من جانب الأكبر، وهذا بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لا بالنسبة بين الطرفين.
[ث] وأما الضرب الرابع فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويا له ولا أخص منه ولا أعم أيضًا كما في الثالث. فأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فلا يمكن أن يكون أخص منه ولا أعم ولا غير الأعم من الأربعة المذكورة ضرورة / يتحقق المباينة بينه وبين الأكبر على ما عرف. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يقع هذا الضرب بتلك النسبة إلا على أربعة أوجه وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الواحد من جانب الأكبر: فهذه المنطقة الكبيرة هي الوجوه المعتبرة باعتبار نسبة الأوسط إلى كل واحد من طرفي الأصغر والأكبر لا باعتبار الغير وهو نسبة أحد الطرفين إلى الآخر.
On four faces according to this relation, and in the relation of the middle to each one of the two extremes, not in the relation of each one of the two extremes to one another. [B] As for the second figure, the middle in it may be more general than the minor, or it may not be, rather it may be equal; and it cannot be more general than the major or equal, and there must be a necessary disparity between it and the major, otherwise it cannot be denied of it. And if it is so, then it can only occur in two faces. And this is according to the relation mentioned, which is the relation of the middle to the two extremes. [T] As for the third figure, the middle in it in relation to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be, rather be equal to it, or it may be neither equal nor more specific nor more general likewise. And as for its relation to the major, it may be either more specific than it or equal. So inevitably this figure occurs in eight faces; which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the two from the side of the major. And this is according to the relation mentioned, not according to the relation between the two extremes. [Th] As for the fourth figure, the middle in it in relation to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be, rather be equal to it, or it may be neither equal nor more specific nor more general, as in the third. As for its relation to the major, it cannot be more specific than it nor more general nor other than the four mentioned, necessarily establishing a disparity between it and the major as known. And if it is so, then this figure cannot occur according to that relation except in four faces, which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the one from the side of the major. So these are the considered faces according to the relation of the middle to each one of the two extremes, the minor and the major, not according to something else, which is the relation of one extreme to the other.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the possible relations (more general, more specific, equal, or disparate) between the middle term and each of the extreme terms (minor and major). The author proceeds figure by figure. For the second figure (where the middle is the predicate of both premises), the middle must be more general than or equal to the minor, but cannot be more general than or equal to the major; it must be disparate from the major. Therefore, only two valid relations (faces) are possible. For the third figure (where the middle is the subject of both premises), the middle can be more general, more specific, equal, or unrelated to the minor, but with respect to the major it can only be more specific or equal; thus eight faces result from the combination. For the fourth figure (where the middle is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major), the middle's relation to the minor is the same four as the third figure, but to the major it is necessarily disparate, so only four faces are possible. The author emphasizes that these considerations are based on the relation of the middle to each extreme individually, not on the relation between the extremes themselves. In kalam and fiqh, understanding these relations helps in constructing valid arguments; for example, in a kalam argument for the existence of a creator, the middle term might be 'temporally originated', which is more general than the minor 'world' and more specific than the major 'created'.

Key Takeaway
The second figure's valid faces are limited to two because the middle must be more general than the minor and necessarily disparate from the major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Verify that the middle term 'forbidden' is more general than 'impurity' and disparate from 'water', and confirm that only two faces are possible.

Third Figure: Eight Valid Faces
Author describes the third figure constraints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the possible relations of the middle term to the minor and major in the third figure and compute its eight valid faces.

Core Idea
Third figure allows four relations to the minor and two to the major, yielding eight faces.

In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. This provides more flexibility in its relation to the minor, but restricts its relation to the major. We'll see how four possibilities on one side and two on the other combine to give eight valid faces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the middle term in the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. This gives it more flexibility in relation to the minor (subject of the conclusion).
The middle is subject in both premises.
2
Determine possible relations between middle and minor
The author lists four possibilities: more general, more specific, equal, or none of these (i.e., disparity). So four options for the minor side.
These are the four standard logical relations (عموم, خصوص, مساواة, مباينة).
3
Determine possible relations between middle and major
The middle can only be more specific than the major or equal to it. It cannot be more general or disparate.
Because the middle is subject of the major premise, and for the syllogism to be valid, the major must be at least as general as the middle.
4
Compute the number of faces
Four possibilities for the minor side times two for the major side equals eight valid faces.

Fourth Figure: Four Valid Faces
Author describes the fourth figure constraints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the constraints on the middle term in the fourth figure and compute its four valid faces.

Core Idea
Fourth figure restricts the middle to be necessarily disparate from the major, limiting faces to four.

The fourth figure has the middle term as predicate of the minor premise and subject of the major premise. This mixing imposes a unique restriction: the middle must be disparate from the major. Consequently, only four valid faces are possible, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the middle term in the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. This asymmetric role leads to the constraints.
Middle is predicate in the minor, subject in the major.
2
Determine possible relations between middle and minor
The middle can be more general, more specific, equal, or disparate in relation to the minor. Same four as the third figure for the minor side.
3
Determine possible relations between middle and major
The author states the middle cannot be more specific, more general, or equal to the major; it must be disparate. This is because of the necessary disparity to ensure a valid conclusion.
Only one possibility: disparity.
4
Compute the number of faces
Four possibilities for the minor side times one for the major side equals four valid faces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أربعة أوجه بهذه النسبة، وفي نسبة الأوسط إلى كل واحد من الطرفين لا بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين إلى صاحبه.
[ب] وأما الضرب الثاني فالأوسط فيه قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون أعم من الأكبر أو مساويًا، وقد كان بينه وبين الأكبر مباينة بالضرورة وإلا لا يمكن أن يسلب عنه، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يقع الا على وجهين، وهذا بالنسبة التي مر ذكرها وهي نسبة الأوسط إلى الطرفين (۱).
[ت] وأما الضرب الثالث فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه ، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويًا ولا أخص منه ولا أعم كذلك. وأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فإما أن يكون أخص منه، أو مساويًا. فلا جرم يقع هذا الضرب على ثمانية أوجه؛ وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الاثنين من جانب الأكبر، وهذا بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لا بالنسبة بين الطرفين.
[ث] وأما الضرب الرابع فالأوسط فيه بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا له، أو لا يكون مساويا له ولا أخص منه ولا أعم أيضًا كما في الثالث. فأما بالنسبة إلى الأكبر فلا يمكن أن يكون أخص منه ولا أعم ولا غير الأعم من الأربعة المذكورة ضرورة / يتحقق المباينة بينه وبين الأكبر على ما عرف. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يقع هذا الضرب بتلك النسبة إلا على أربعة أوجه وهي الحاصل من ضرب الأربعة المذكورة من جانب الأصغر في الواحد من جانب الأكبر: فهذه المنطقة الكبيرة هي الوجوه المعتبرة باعتبار نسبة الأوسط إلى كل واحد من طرفي الأصغر والأكبر لا باعتبار الغير وهو نسبة أحد الطرفين إلى الآخر.
On four faces according to this relation, and in the relation of the middle to each one of the two extremes, not in the relation of each one of the two extremes to one another. [B] As for the second figure, the middle in it may be more general than the minor, or it may not be, rather it may be equal; and it cannot be more general than the major or equal, and there must be a necessary disparity between it and the major, otherwise it cannot be denied of it. And if it is so, then it can only occur in two faces. And this is according to the relation mentioned, which is the relation of the middle to the two extremes. [T] As for the third figure, the middle in it in relation to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be, rather be equal to it, or it may be neither equal nor more specific nor more general likewise. And as for its relation to the major, it may be either more specific than it or equal. So inevitably this figure occurs in eight faces; which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the two from the side of the major. And this is according to the relation mentioned, not according to the relation between the two extremes. [Th] As for the fourth figure, the middle in it in relation to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or it may not be, rather be equal to it, or it may be neither equal nor more specific nor more general, as in the third. As for its relation to the major, it cannot be more specific than it nor more general nor other than the four mentioned, necessarily establishing a disparity between it and the major as known. And if it is so, then this figure cannot occur according to that relation except in four faces, which is the product of multiplying the four mentioned from the side of the minor by the one from the side of the major. So these are the considered faces according to the relation of the middle to each one of the two extremes, the minor and the major, not according to something else, which is the relation of one extreme to the other.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the possible relations (more general, more specific, equal, or disparate) between the middle term and each of the extreme terms (minor and major). The author proceeds figure by figure. For the second figure (where the middle is the predicate of both premises), the middle must be more general than or equal to the minor, but cannot be more general than or equal to the major; it must be disparate from the major. Therefore, only two valid relations (faces) are possible. For the third figure (where the middle is the subject of both premises), the middle can be more general, more specific, equal, or unrelated to the minor, but with respect to the major it can only be more specific or equal; thus eight faces result from the combination. For the fourth figure (where the middle is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major), the middle's relation to the minor is the same four as the third figure, but to the major it is necessarily disparate, so only four faces are possible. The author emphasizes that these considerations are based on the relation of the middle to each extreme individually, not on the relation between the extremes themselves. In kalam and fiqh, understanding these relations helps in constructing valid arguments; for example, in a kalam argument for the existence of a creator, the middle term might be 'temporally originated', which is more general than the minor 'world' and more specific than the major 'created'.

Identifying Valid Moods in the First Figure by Middle Term Unity
Enumerates and reduces mood combinations by middle term identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of any first-figure syllogism based on the affirmative/negative quality of its premises and the identity of the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term must be the exact same term in both premises.

So far, we have examined syllogistic figures based on the arrangement of terms. Now we add another layer: the quality of the premises—whether they are affirmative or negative. The author shows that not every combination of an affirmative or negative minor with an affirmative or negative major yields a valid syllogism. The key constraint is that the middle term must be identical in both premises. This lesson will guide you through the 16 possible combinations and show you how to eliminate the invalid ones by checking for middle term unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality of the minor premise
Determine if the minor premise is affirmative (مُحَصَّلَة) or negative (مَعْدُولَة). If negative, specify which part is negated: subject only, predicate only, or both.
The author lists three types of negative propositions.
2
Identify the quality of the major premise
Similarly, classify the major premise as affirmative or one of the three negative types.
3
Check whether the middle term appears identically
Compare the middle term as it appears in the minor premise with how it appears in the major premise. They must be exactly the same term; if one is negated and the other is not, the middle is not unified. For example, if the minor has 'B' and the major has 'not B', they differ.
This is the condition of اتحاد الوسط (unity of the middle).
4
Apply the pattern to eliminate invalid combinations
Using the examples given: if the minor is affirmative, the major cannot have a negated subject or both negated. If the minor is negated in subject, the major cannot have a negated subject or both. If the minor is negated in predicate, the major cannot be affirmative or negated in predicate. If the minor is both negated, the major cannot be affirmative or negated in predicate. This leaves 8 valid moods out of 16.
Memorize these restrictions for the first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أن كل واحد من الضروب الأربعة متعددة بحسب العموم والخصوص كما مر فكذلك بحسب العدول والتحصيل. وذلك لأن الصغرى [١] قد تكون محصلة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون معدولة؛ والمعدولة [٢] إما معدولة الموضوع فقط، [۳] أو معدولة المحمول فقط، [٤] أو معدولة الموضوع والمحمول فهي إذن أربعة وكذلك الكبرى فإنها قد تكون محصلة وقد لا تكون بل تكون معدولة كما مر . والحاصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة؛ ستة عشر.
[1] فكذلك الوجوه الواقعة في الضرب الأول من الضروب الأربعة لكن الثمانية منها ساقطة لتعذر اتحاد الوسط، والصغرى إذا كانت محصلة فلا يمكن أن تكون كبراها معدولة الموضوع ولا معدولة الموضوع والمحمول أيضًا. فإن محمول الصغرى في هذين القسمين محصل، وموضوع الكبرى فيهما معدول؛ فيقال مثلا كل (ج) ب) ، وكل ما ليس (ب) فهو «أ»، أو يقال كل ج ب ، وكل ما ليس (ب) فهو ليس «أ»، فالأوسط لا يكون متحدا فيهما فإن من المحال أن يكون اب متحدا مع ما ليس «ب». وعلى هذا إذا كانت الصغرى معدولة الموضوع فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون كبراها معدولة الموضوع ولا معدولة الموضوع والمحمول أيضًا. وكذلك إذا كانت معدولة المحمول فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون كبراها محصلة، ولا معدولة المحمول كذلك. وكذلك إذا كانت معدولة الموضوع والمحمول فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون كبراها محصلة، ولا معدولة المحمول أيضًا. ولو كانت الحالة هذه لكان اتحاد الوسط لا يمكن إلا في الثمانية الباقية؛ والاتحاد من اللوازم على ما عرف وكانت الوجوه المعتبرة من الوجوه الستة عشر ثمانية، والبواقي ساقطة.
And just as each one of the four figures is multiple according to generality and particularity as mentioned, so too according to negation and affirmation. That is because the minor premise [1] may be affirmative, or it may be negative; and the negative [2] is either negative in subject only, [3] or negative in predicate only, [4] or negative in both subject and predicate; so it is then four. Likewise the major premise, for it may be affirmative or it may be negative as mentioned. The product of multiplying four by four is sixteen. [1] So likewise the faces occurring in the first figure of the four figures, but eight of them are invalid due to the impossibility of the unity of the middle term. For if the minor premise is affirmative, then its major cannot be negative in subject nor negative in both subject and predicate. For the predicate of the minor in these two cases is affirmative, and the subject of the major in them is negative; thus it is said, for example, 'Every J is B' and 'Everything that is not B is A', or it is said 'Every J is B' and 'Everything that is not B is not A'. So the middle is not unified in them, for it is impossible that B is identical with what is not B. And likewise, if the minor is negative in subject, then its major cannot be negative in subject nor negative in both subject and predicate. And likewise, if it is negative in predicate, then its major cannot be affirmative nor negative in predicate likewise. And likewise, if it is negative in both subject and predicate, then its major cannot be affirmative nor negative in predicate also. If this is the case, then unity of the middle is possible only in the remaining eight. And unity is necessary as known. So the considered faces from the sixteen are eight, and the rest are invalid.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage extends the previous classification by considering the quality of the premises: affirmative (محصلة) or negative (معدولة). The author first divides negative propositions into three types: those with a negated subject, negated predicate, or both. This gives four possible forms for each premise (one affirmative and three negative), leading to 4 × 4 = 16 combinations for the first figure (to which this discussion is limited, as indicated by 'the faces occurring in the first figure'). However, not all 16 are valid. The condition for validity is that the middle term must be identical in both premises (اتحاد الوسط). For example, if the minor is affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B'), and the major has a negated subject (e.g., 'Everything that is not B is A'), then the middle term appears as 'B' in the minor and 'not B' in the major, so they are not the same term, rendering the syllogism invalid. The author systematically examines each case: when the minor is affirmative, the major cannot have a negated subject or both negated; when the minor is negated in subject, the major cannot have a negated subject or both; when the minor is negated in predicate, the major cannot be affirmative or negated in predicate; when the minor is both negated, the major cannot be affirmative or negated in predicate. This eliminates 8 combinations, leaving 8 valid ones. In fiqh, for example, such distinctions are crucial: a syllogism like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden' uses affirmative premises. If one premise were negative in a way that breaks the middle term identity, the argument would be unsound.

Key Takeaway
When constructing or evaluating a first-figure syllogism, always verify that the middle term appears identically in both premises; otherwise the argument is unsound.

Valid Premise Combinations in the Second Figure
Author applies the same combinatorial analysis to the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise combinations in the second figure yield valid categorical syllogisms, given the major premise is universally negative.

Core Idea
The second figure's negative major still permits 8 valid combinations, just like the first figure.

In this lesson, we extend our analysis from the first figure to the second. The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises, and the major premise is universally negative. As in the first figure, each premise can take four forms (affirmative, negative subject, negative predicate, or both). The same logical condition—that the middle term must be distributed in the major premise—applies, yielding again 8 valid combinations out of 16. The author refers back to the first figure's reasoning, so you will need to recall that principle.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four forms of a proposition
A categorical proposition can be simple (affirmative or negative) or have the negation attached to the subject (معدولة الموضوع), the predicate (معدولة المحمول), or both (معدولة الموضوع والمحمول). The minor premise in the second figure can take all these forms, just like in the first figure.
2
Identify the major premise's forms
Although the major premise in the second figure is always negative, it too has four variants: simple negative (بسيطة), negative subject, negative predicate, and both. So the major can be any of these four.
The term 'بسيطة' here means a simple negative with negation only on the copula.
3
Compute total combinations
Since the minor has 4 forms and the major has 4 forms, there are 4 × 4 = 16 possible combinations of premise forms.
4
Apply the middle term distribution condition
For a syllogism to be valid, the middle term must be distributed in at least one premise. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. Since the major is negative, its predicate (the middle) is distributed. Therefore, any combination where the major is negative (which it always is) automatically satisfies the distribution condition. However, other conditions (like the conclusion must follow from the premises) reduce the valid count to 8, as per the detailed analysis in the first figure.
The author asserts that 8 combinations are valid, mirroring the first figure, without re-proving it.
5
Verify with an example
Consider a valid second-figure syllogism: 'No stone is an animal (major, simple negative). Every animal is a body (minor, affirmative). Therefore, some body is not a stone.' This has the form Cesare (EIO) and is valid. The major is negative simple, minor affirmative – one of the 8 valid combos.
The example uses Islamic science material: stones, animals, bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الثاني من الضروب الأربعة فإن الصغرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول كما مرَّ في الضرب الأول. وكذلك الكبرى فإنها وإن كانت سالبة فقد كانت على أربعة أوجه لما أنها قد تكون بسيطة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول؛ فتكون الوجوه الواقعة فيه ستة عشر أيضًا : ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية منها باقية والثمانية الباقية من الوجوه المعتبرة كما مرَّ .
And in the second figure of the four figures, the minor premise may be affirmative, may be negative in subject, may be negative in predicate, and may be negative in subject and predicate as passed in the first figure. And likewise the major premise, for although it is negative, it has four aspects: it may be simple, may be negative in subject, may be negative in predicate, and may be negative in subject and predicate. Thus the aspects occurring in it are also sixteen: eight of them are invalid, and eight of them are valid, and the remaining eight are the considered aspects as passed.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage applies the same analytical method to the second figure. The major premise in the second figure is universally negative (سالبة كلية), but it too has four possible forms: simple (بسيطة) and three types of negative-modified (معدولة الموضوع, معدولة المحمول, معدولة الموضوع والمحمول). The minor premise has the same four forms as before. Thus, as in the first figure, there are 16 possible combinations of minor and major premise qualities. By the same reasoning (middle term identity), 8 are valid and 8 are invalid. The author does not repeat the detailed justification but says 'as passed' (كما مرَّ). This shows that the underlying condition applies uniformly across figures, regardless of the specific quantity and quality of the figure.

Key Takeaway

Third Figure: Valid Premise Combinations with Particular Minor
Author applies the analysis to the third figure, where the minor is particular affirmative, yielding 8 valid combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which premise quality-modifications in the third figure yield valid syllogisms.

Core Idea
The third figure's 8 valid combinations follow from the same middle term identity principle as the first figure.

In this lesson, we analyze the third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major is universal affirmative. The author notes that by varying the quality modification of each premise, the total possibilities are 16, of which 8 are valid. We'll see how this result is derived from the same principle as in the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premise types
The third figure has a particular affirmative minor (e.g., 'Some J is B') and a universal affirmative major (e.g., 'Every C is B'). Note the middle term (B) is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
2
List quality modifications for each premise
Each premise has four forms: pure affirmative (محصلة), negated subject (معدولة الموضوع), negated predicate (معدولة المحمول), and both negated (معدولة الموضوع والمحمول). Write these for both minor and major.
Negated subject/predicate means the term is replaced by its negation, e.g., 'Some J is non-B'.
3
Compute total combinations
Multiply the 4 forms of the minor by the 4 forms of the major to get 16 possible premise pairs. This matches the number in the first figure.
4
Apply the middle term distribution condition
For a valid syllogism, the middle term must be distributed at least once. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor. Check which modifications distribute it. The same reasoning as in the first figure yields 8 valid combinations.
Modifications that negate the subject of the major or the predicate of the minor affect distribution.
5
Verify the 8 valid combinations
The author states that 8 are valid and 8 are invalid. The valid ones are those where the middle term is distributed in at least one premise. This ensures the syllogism is sound.
You can test an example: Minor: 'Some animal is rational' (affirmative), Major: 'Every rational is laughing' (affirmative) → valid. If minor becomes 'Some animal is non-rational', the middle term 'rational' is not distributed, so invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الثالث فإن الصغرى وهي الموجبة الجزئية قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول وكذلك الكبرى وهي الموجبة الكلية كما مرَّ في الضرب الأول فتكون الوجوه الواقعة ستة عشر أيضًا، ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية باقية.
And in the third figure, the minor premise, which is the particular affirmative, may be affirmative, may be negative in subject, may be negative in predicate, and may be negative in subject and predicate. And likewise the major premise, which is the universal affirmative, as passed in the first figure. Thus the aspects occurring are also sixteen, eight of them invalid, and eight valid.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage covers the third figure. Its distinguishing feature is that the minor premise is particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية) and the major premise is universal affirmative (موجبة كلية). The author refers back to the first figure for the detailed justification.

Fourth Figure: Valid Premise Combinations with Negative Major
Author applies the analysis to the fourth figure, with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding 8 valid combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of valid moods in the fourth figure given the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative.

Core Idea
The fourth figure mirrors the same 8-of-16 pattern as other figures.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. The author applies the same analysis as before, noting that the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative. Despite the change in quantity and quality, the same combinatorial logic applies, yielding 8 valid moods out of 16 possible combinations. Understanding this pattern is crucial for quickly recognizing valid syllogisms in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed premise types
In the fourth figure, the minor is particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية) and the major is universal negative (سالبة كلية). Both can be in affirmative (محصلة) or modified (معدولة) forms.
Each premise has four possible forms: affirmative and three types of negative-modified.
2
Enumerate total combinations
Since each premise has 4 forms, total combinations = 4 × 4 = 16. List them to see the full set.
3
Apply the middle term identity condition
The middle term must be taken identically in both premises. This condition eliminates half the combinations, leaving 8 valid.
The author states 'as passed' indicating the same reasoning from earlier figures applies.
4
Conclude valid count
Thus, 8 out of 16 combinations are valid (باقية) and 8 are invalid (ساقطة). This matches the first, second, and third figures.

Binary vs Ternary Propositions: The Copula in Categorical Syllogisms
Author introduces another dimension of variation: binary vs. ternary propositional forms (with or without copula).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish binary from ternary propositions and identify the presence or absence of the copula 'هو' in categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
The copula 'هو' can be explicit (ternary) or implicit (binary), but both forms are logically equivalent.

In the previous lesson, we learned that premises vary by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). The author now introduces another dimension: the syntactic form of the proposition. A proposition can be 'binary' (ثنائية), meaning it has no explicit copula, like 'كل ج ب', or 'ternary' (ثلاثية), meaning it includes the copula 'هو', like 'كل ج هو ب'. Although the passage cuts off, this distinction is important for understanding the formal structure of syllogisms and how the middle term is expressed.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subject and predicate
Identify the subject (مبتدأ) and predicate (خبر) in the proposition. In 'كل ج ب', the subject is 'ج' and predicate is 'ب'.
2
Check for the copula 'هو'
Look for an explicit word 'هو' (or 'هي' for feminine) between the subject and predicate. If present, the proposition is ternary; if absent, it is binary.
In Arabic, the copula is often omitted in simple statements.
3
Test with examples
Practice: 'كل مسكر حرام' is binary; 'كل مسكر هو حرام' is ternary. Both mean the same logically.
4
Apply to syllogisms
In a syllogism, both the major and minor premises can be binary or ternary independently. The author notes that this creates further variation within each figure.
The passage begins to discuss combinations (e.g., minor binary without major), but is cut off.

Sixteen Combinations in the Second Figure
Author details the sixteen combinations in the second figure and specifies which are valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the sixteen premise combinations in the second figure and identify which eight are valid using the established criteria.

Core Idea
The second figure yields eight valid moods, mirroring the first figure in its ratio.

In the second figure, the minor premise is affirmative and the major is negative. Each premise can appear in four qualified forms (simple, negated subject, negated predicate, both negated). Multiplying the possibilities gives sixteen combinations. The author now specifies that exactly half are valid, just as in the first figure. Understanding this enumeration is crucial for mastering the moods of categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first-figure enumeration
The author refers to the previous discussion of the first figure where the minor premise had four forms (simple, negated subject, negated predicate, both negated) and the major had four forms, yielding sixteen total, eight valid. This pattern is now applied to the second figure.
This parallelism is intentional; the author uses the same method for each figure.
2
Identify the forms of the minor premise
Since the second figure requires an affirmative minor, the minor can be (1) simple affirmative, (2) affirmative with negated subject, (3) affirmative with negated predicate, or (4) affirmative with both subject and predicate negated. Each form is a distinct possibility.
These are called 'qualified propositions' (معدولة).
3
Identify the forms of the major premise
The major premise is negative (as per second figure conditions). It also has four forms: (1) simple negative, (2) negative with negated subject, (3) negative with negated predicate, (4) negative with both negated.
Thus the major, though negative, still undergoes the same qualification variations.
4
Compute the total combinations
Multiply the four minor forms by the four major forms: 4 × 4 = 16 possible combinations (وجوه واقعة).
5
Separate valid from invalid
According to the author, eight of these combinations are valid (باقية) and eight are invalid (ساقطة). This is the same split as in the first figure. The valid ones are those that satisfy the conditions of the second figure: major universal negative, minor affirmative, and the conclusion is negative with proper distribution.
The details of which eight are valid are not given here but follow from the first-figure criteria applied to the second figure.

Binary and Ternary Premises in Syllogisms
Author introduces further subdivisions of premises into binary and ternary forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify binary and ternary premises in a categorical syllogism and enumerate the four combinations for each figure.

Core Idea
Binary premises omit the copula; ternary premises include it — but both are logically equivalent.

After learning the basic structure of categorical syllogisms, the author now introduces a new way of subdividing premises: by whether the copula 'is' is explicitly present. This distinction, though seemingly linguistic, affects how syllogisms are written in Arabic logical texts. The passage explains that each premise in any figure can be binary (two terms, no copula) or ternary (three terms including copula), giving four variations per figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms in a binary vs ternary premise
A binary premise has only two terms written without a copula, e.g., 'كل ج ب'. A ternary premise includes an explicit copula 'هو', e.g., 'كل ج هو ب'. Practice identifying the subject and predicate in each.
2
Classify a given premise as binary or ternary
Look for the presence or absence of 'هو'. If 'هو' appears between the two terms, it is ternary; otherwise binary.
Some texts may use other copula forms like 'موجود' but here it's specifically 'هو'.
3
Enumerate the four combinations per figure
For any figure, the major and minor premise can each be binary or ternary, yielding four possibilities: both binary, both ternary, minor binary major ternary, minor ternary major binary.
The author states this explicitly: 'الوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب من الضروب أربعة'.
4
Apply to a real syllogism example
Take a first-figure syllogism: 'كل جسم مؤلف' (binary) and 'كل مؤلف محدث' (ternary with copula). Recognize that the first premise is binary and the second ternary, so it falls under the combination 'minor binary, major ternary'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أن كل واحد من الضروب الأربعة متعددة بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل كما تقدم فكذلك بحسب الغير. وذلك أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى في كل ضرب من الضروب [١] إما أن تكون ثنائية كما يقال كل ج) ب»، وكل «أب»؛ [٢] وإما أن تكون ثلاثية كما يقال كل (ج) هو «ب»، وكل «ب» هو «أ»؛ [۳] وإما أن تكون الصغرى ثنائية دون الكبرى [٤] أو بالعكس، فتكون الوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب من الضروب أربعة بهذا الاعتبار.
And just as each of the four figures is multiple according to universality/particularity and qualification/simplicity as passed, likewise according to another aspect. That is, each of the minor and major in every figure [1] is either binary, as when one says 'Every J is B' and 'Every A is B'; [2] or ternary, as when one says 'Every J is B' and 'Every B is A'; [3] or the minor is binary without the major [4] or the opposite. So the combinations occurring in each figure are four in this respect.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage introduces a new dimension for varying premises: whether they are expressed in a 'binary' form (two terms without copula) or 'ternary' (with a copula like 'is'). This gives four combinations per figure (both binary, both ternary, first binary second ternary, first ternary second binary). This multiplies the previous counts.

Calculating the Vast Number of Syllogistic Combinations
Combinatorial calculation of syllogistic possibilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the number of possible premise combinations in the first figure given quantity, quality, and qualification dimensions.

Core Idea
The number of possible syllogistic forms is staggeringly large, but only a few are valid.

The author takes a combinatorial approach to show the immense scope of syllogistic possibility. By multiplying the four types of quantity/quality by eight types of qualification/simplicity, he demonstrates that the first figure alone yields 128 combinations. This calculation highlights the need for strict conditions to isolate the valid moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dimensions
The author considers two dimensions: universality/particularity (4 types) and qualification/simplicity (8 types). In the first figure, the four types from the first dimension are multiplied by the eight from the second.
The four types are: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. The eight types from qualification/simplicity are derived from the possible combinations of affirmative/negative with simple/qualified.
2
Calculate the first figure total
Multiply 4 (from universality/particularity) by 8 (from qualification/simplicity) to get 128 combinations for the first figure.
The author states: 'بلغت الوجوه الواقعة في الضرب الأول مبلغ مائة وثمانية وعشرين'.
3
Extend to other figures
Note the pattern: second figure has half (64) because it has only 2 in universality/particularity; third figure has double (256) because it has 8; fourth figure equals the first (128). Multiplying further by simple and mixed syllogisms yields enormous numbers.
This serves to show the vastness of syllogistic possibilities, emphasizing the need for rigorous conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو ضربت الأربعة الواقعة [1] في الضرب الأول بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل فيه؛ والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك بلغت الوجوه الواقعة في الضرب الأول مبلغ مائة وثمانية وعشرين؛ [ب] وفي الضرب الثاني بلغت مبلغ النصف من هذا؛ إذ فيه اثنان بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ [ت] وفي الثالث مبلغ الضعف؛ إذ فيه ثمانية بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ وذلك مائتان وستة وخمسون؛ [ث] وفي الرابع مبلغ النصف من هذا وهو مثل ما في الأول. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه بأجمعها في جميع أقسام الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغاً كثيرًا أضعاف هذه المبالغ لا محالة. ولا يبعد أن يخطر ببال أحد أن المنتج من هذا الشكل لا يكون منحصرا في هذه الضروب الأربعة؛ إذ السالبة الكلية إذا كانت صغرى الموجبة الكلية أو الجزئية كما يقال لا شيء من (ج) ب)، وكل ب أ ينتج ليس كل «أج»، فإن الكبرى إذا عكست ينتج من الشكل الثاني ليس كل «أج» . وذلك لأنها تنعكس جزئية، والجزئية لا تصلح للكبروية في ذلك الشكل فيكون صغراه هكذا بعض «أب»، ولا شيء من «ج» «ب» فينتج ليس بعض أج» . وكذلك إذا كانت السالبة الكلية صغرى الموجبة الجزئية. ثمَّ إنه وما هو بمثله أيضًا فذلك من الوهميات؛ ورفعها بالنظر في قولنا إن من الشرائط أن تكون الصغرى موجبة في الشكل الأول؛ إذ الصغرى فيه ما فيه الأصغر الذي يجب أن يكون موضوع النتيجة وهو «ج»، فإذا جعلناه موضوع النتيجة وحملنا عليه «أ» لم يلزم منه البتة ليس بعض «ج أ». فإذن إن كان ينتج ما ذكر تم فليس ذلك من الصغرى والكبرى على ما وضع وكيف
If you multiply the four occurring in the first figure according to universality/particularity by the eight remaining which are according to qualification/simplicity in it, and the total in these four likewise, the combinations occurring in the first figure reach one hundred and twenty-eight. [B] In the second figure it reaches half of that, for it has two according to universality/particularity. [T] In the third it reaches double, for it has eight according to universality/particularity, that is two hundred and fifty-six. [Th] In the fourth it reaches half of that, equal to the first. Then if you multiply all these combinations by all the divisions of simple and mixed syllogisms, they become a very large number, many times these totals, without doubt. And it is not far from someone's mind that the productive moods of this figure are not confined to these four; for a universal negative if it is a minor premise with a universal affirmative or particular (as when it is said 'No J is B, and every B is A') yields 'Not every A is J', for if the major is converted it yields from the second figure 'Not every A is J'. That is because it converts to particular, and a particular is not suitable as a major in that figure. So its minor would be 'Some A is B', and 'No J is B' yields 'Not some A is J'. Likewise if the universal negative is minor with the affirmative particular. Then that and what is like it are among illusions; and their refutation by considering our statement that one of the conditions is that the minor be affirmative in the first figure; for the minor in it contains the smallest term which must be the subject of the conclusion, which is 'J'. So if we make it the subject of the conclusion and predicate 'A' upon it, it does not at all necessitate 'Not some J is A'. Therefore if it produces what was mentioned, it is not from the minor and major as placed and how.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is twofold. First, it calculates the huge number of possible premise combinations when multiplying all dimensions (quantity, quality, qualification, binary/ternary). Then it addresses an objection: one might think the first figure has more than four moods because a syllogism like 'No J is B, every B is A' seems to produce 'Not every A is J'. The author refutes this by noting that the minor in the first figure must be affirmative. In the alleged example, the minor is negative, so it does not belong to the first figure. The conclusion claimed is not valid in the first figure's form. Hence, the four moods remain the only valid ones.

If a jurist must consider all combinations of a ruling's attributes (e.g., 3 attributes each with 2 states), how many total combinations exist? Use the combinatorial method from this lesson.
