The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Why the First Figure Has Exactly Four Valid Moods
Refutation of an objection to the first figure's moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that a universal negative minor in the first figure can produce a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
The first figure's minor must be affirmative; a negative minor yields only illusions.

Someone might object that a syllogism like 'No J is B, every B is A' seems to produce 'Not every A is J'. The author refutes this by invoking the essential condition that the minor premise in the first figure must be affirmative. He shows that the alleged conclusion does not follow from the proper arrangement, and the argument collapses into an illusion.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector proposes: 'No J is B, every B is A, therefore not every A is J.' This appears to be a valid first-figure syllogism with a universal negative minor.
In logical notation: ¬(J⊆B) ∧ (B⊆A) ∴ ¬(A⊆J).
2
Identify the figure violation
In the first figure, the minor premise (containing the subject of the conclusion) must be affirmative. Here the minor is negative ('No J is B'), so the syllogism does not belong to the first figure.
The author states: 'من الشرائط أن تكون الصغرى موجبة في الشكل الأول'.
3
Analyze the conversion attempt
The objector tries to convert the major premise: 'Every B is A' becomes 'Some A is B' (accidental conversion). Then with 'No J is B', they attempt a second-figure syllogism.
Conversion: 'كل ب أ' becomes 'بعض أ ب'. This is valid for affirmative universal to particular affirmative.
4
Show the conversion fails
The converted major 'Some A is B' is particular, but the second figure requires a universal major. Thus the argument is invalid as a second-figure syllogism.
Second figure condition: major premise must be universal.
5
Conclude with the author's ruling
The author calls such arguments 'wahmiyyāt' (illusions) and reaffirms that the first figure has only four valid moods, all requiring an affirmative minor.
The four moods: Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio. Note: Ferio has a negative major, but minor is still affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو ضربت الأربعة الواقعة [1] في الضرب الأول بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل فيه؛ والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك بلغت الوجوه الواقعة في الضرب الأول مبلغ مائة وثمانية وعشرين؛ [ب] وفي الضرب الثاني بلغت مبلغ النصف من هذا؛ إذ فيه اثنان بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ [ت] وفي الثالث مبلغ الضعف؛ إذ فيه ثمانية بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ وذلك مائتان وستة وخمسون؛ [ث] وفي الرابع مبلغ النصف من هذا وهو مثل ما في الأول. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه بأجمعها في جميع أقسام الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغاً كثيرًا أضعاف هذه المبالغ لا محالة. ولا يبعد أن يخطر ببال أحد أن المنتج من هذا الشكل لا يكون منحصرا في هذه الضروب الأربعة؛ إذ السالبة الكلية إذا كانت صغرى الموجبة الكلية أو الجزئية كما يقال لا شيء من (ج) ب)، وكل ب أ ينتج ليس كل «أج»، فإن الكبرى إذا عكست ينتج من الشكل الثاني ليس كل «أج» . وذلك لأنها تنعكس جزئية، والجزئية لا تصلح للكبروية في ذلك الشكل فيكون صغراه هكذا بعض «أب»، ولا شيء من «ج» «ب» فينتج ليس بعض أج» . وكذلك إذا كانت السالبة الكلية صغرى الموجبة الجزئية. ثمَّ إنه وما هو بمثله أيضًا فذلك من الوهميات؛ ورفعها بالنظر في قولنا إن من الشرائط أن تكون الصغرى موجبة في الشكل الأول؛ إذ الصغرى فيه ما فيه الأصغر الذي يجب أن يكون موضوع النتيجة وهو «ج»، فإذا جعلناه موضوع النتيجة وحملنا عليه «أ» لم يلزم منه البتة ليس بعض «ج أ». فإذن إن كان ينتج ما ذكر تم فليس ذلك من الصغرى والكبرى على ما وضع وكيف
If you multiply the four occurring in the first figure according to universality/particularity by the eight remaining which are according to qualification/simplicity in it, and the total in these four likewise, the combinations occurring in the first figure reach one hundred and twenty-eight. [B] In the second figure it reaches half of that, for it has two according to universality/particularity. [T] In the third it reaches double, for it has eight according to universality/particularity, that is two hundred and fifty-six. [Th] In the fourth it reaches half of that, equal to the first. Then if you multiply all these combinations by all the divisions of simple and mixed syllogisms, they become a very large number, many times these totals, without doubt. And it is not far from someone's mind that the productive moods of this figure are not confined to these four; for a universal negative if it is a minor premise with a universal affirmative or particular (as when it is said 'No J is B, and every B is A') yields 'Not every A is J', for if the major is converted it yields from the second figure 'Not every A is J'. That is because it converts to particular, and a particular is not suitable as a major in that figure. So its minor would be 'Some A is B', and 'No J is B' yields 'Not some A is J'. Likewise if the universal negative is minor with the affirmative particular. Then that and what is like it are among illusions; and their refutation by considering our statement that one of the conditions is that the minor be affirmative in the first figure; for the minor in it contains the smallest term which must be the subject of the conclusion, which is 'J'. So if we make it the subject of the conclusion and predicate 'A' upon it, it does not at all necessitate 'Not some J is A'. Therefore if it produces what was mentioned, it is not from the minor and major as placed and how.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is twofold. First, it calculates the huge number of possible premise combinations when multiplying all dimensions (quantity, quality, qualification, binary/ternary). Then it addresses an objection: one might think the first figure has more than four moods because a syllogism like 'No J is B, every B is A' seems to produce 'Not every A is J'. The author refutes this by noting that the minor in the first figure must be affirmative. In the alleged example, the minor is negative, so it does not belong to the first figure. The conclusion claimed is not valid in the first figure's form. Hence, the four moods remain the only valid ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the mood with a universal negative minor in the first figure?
A) The conclusion is not universal
B) The minor must be affirmative
C) The major must be negative
D) The middle term is not distributed

Key Takeaway
The first figure requires an affirmative minor; any syllogism with a negative minor is not a valid first-figure mood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following argument: 'No voluntary act is compelled, and every compelled act is involuntary. Therefore, not every involuntary act is voluntary.' Does this qualify as a valid first-figure syllogism? Why or why not?
?
Identify the condition that makes a first-figure syllogism valid. Construct a valid first-figure syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., rulings on intoxicants) and explain why it satisfies the condition.
